# Introduction  
**Who will help me find my way?**  
The term "wayfaring" bas been In the Englisb language since the  
16tb century. •way-finding" was flTSt used by tbe American arcbi·  
tect Kevin Lyocb In his lnfiueotlal book, *The Image of the Oit11*  
The *Shorter Oxford Engl/sh Dictionary*  
*\(SOED\)* hos the following definitions for  
**analogous words\:**  
\(1960\).'  
Although early in his book Lynch used the term "way-finding  
***Make one's way ,.* To travel or proceed in an**  
**Intended direction or to a certain place.**  
**devices"' to describe maps, street numbers, route signs, and so on,**  
**his work is based on the concept of .. spatial orientation" and its**  
**prerequisite, the cognitive map or. as be cal.ls it, the image.**  
*Wayfarer* \(1440\), A traveller by road.  
**especially one who traveis on foot.**  
We must credit bim as the first person to recognize the impor·  
lance or "imaging" to people f10ding their way. An environmental  
image, be wrote, is the generalized mental representation of the  
external physical world that every individual tries to form. It is the  
***Wayfaring* \(1836\), Travelling or journeying**  
**by road, usually *a wayfaring man,* a traveller**  
by road \(Isa. 35\:8\).  
product or immediate sensation *plus* the memory of past experi·  
eoce, both or which combine to interpret information and guide  
action.  
Perhaps the best known and certainly most quoted part or  
Lynch's book is his analysis or the city in terms or Its elements.  
These be called paths, edges, landmarks. nodes, and districts.  
These are terms tbat continue to be used lo wayflodlng design and  
theory today.  
Lynch had a major lofiueoce on research during the 60s wblch  
abounded lo Investigations Into city Images. Unfortunately, they  
had no Immediate lofiuence on "slgoage and graphics" and very  
little on architecture. It was In the early 70s tbat an Important  
conceptual shift occurred. Cognitivists, such as Steven Kaplan.'  
Roger Downs, and David Stea.' argued that In order to understand  
what people do and how they find their way, one has to understand  
the underlying process. Xbe relevant concept was, therefore, no  
**longer '"spatial orientation" but a new notion incorporating all the**  
**perceptual, cognitive, and decision· making processes necessary to**  
find one's way. This new con cept was baptized "wayf10ding. •  
Over twenty years separste Lynch's book from the pubUcation  
of *Wa11finding in Architecture* in 1984 by my co-author, Romedi  
Passini.• He presented the design profession with the first global  
conceptuallzalion of wayfloding based on empirical data, and he  
ldentllled major difficulties people bad with architecture and  
slgn8gll when finding tbeir way lo complex urban settings.  
He also showed that spatial orientation and wayflodlng have  
fascinated people througb the ages and that this fascination has  
round expression In art and folklore. Most Important however, be  
showed lbal wayfloding was a key design Issue and could no longer  
be ignored.  
It is lair to say that while tbe notion of wayfinding is now  
accepted by many people, it has not yet made its luU impact on the  
design profession. This boo\)\<4 an attemt t to bring this about.

vUNIVERSITY OF MICHIGAN The roles that are currently fulfilled by architects and graphic  
designers are still hostages to the status quo - to that outworn but  
still prevailing notion that arehitects design buildings, while  
graphic designers come along at the end or the building process  
and install some signs. and that the public is somehow helped by  
this. It is admiltedly dlflicult to break out ol this straitjacket, but it  
is Important that we do so.  
Society no longer tolerates the Idea that people's inability to  
find their way around in the built environment is either trivial or  
unimportant. Moreover. there ls ample evidence that society is now  
prepared to satisfy the demands or the disabled by making public  
and commercial faclllties more accessible to them.  
There Is. therefore. an urgent need lor us to escape lrom the  
cyranny of the status quo and to explore new approaches to coping  
with these very real but, until recently, largely unacknowledged  
problems.  
It is the authors' fervent wish that the exampl e of this book will  
create the matrix through which a new proresaion, Waylinding  
Design, can begin to emerge - one that will i . ndced extend the  
boundaries or profcsaional practice in both the arehitectural and  
# the design professions.

**The nature of wayfinding difficulties**  
The emergence of wayllndlng dllllcultlee, one may tbJnk, Is **a**  
recent phenomenon brougbt on by the complexity of contempo-  
nuy buUdlngs and clUes. Nobody can deny that these dllllcul-  
tlee are exacerbated In today's urban setU,.gs, but a UtUe bit  
of digging shows clearly tbat dlfflcultlee with knowing wbere  
one Is, where to go, and bow to get there, aU go beck a very  
lo,.g way. An anthropologlcal view of the labyrinth - wblcb Is  
both the artifact and tbe symbol of being disoriented and lost -  
brl,.gs to Ugbt **a** preoccupation with this s ubject wblcb Is as  
**old as b11manklnd.**  
In tbl.s chapter we Introduce tbe nature of contemponuy  
wayllndlng dllllcultlee. There are so many of them that even  
making **a** represeutatlve choice Is dUllcult.

UNIVERSITY OF MICHIGAN 1.1 A potpoun1 of wayftndlng dlftlcultles  
You, the reader, may just scan tbrougb this chapter to get an over-  
all picture of what the difficulties are. Each issue will be looked at  
in detail later on in the book.  
Put yourself in a travel mood - you are going for an imaginary  
journey. In fact, you are already in a foreign city, and you have to  
catch a train. You are in a bit of a hurry.  
Is lb.ls the railway station?  
You look at a building and wonder whether It is a railway station or  
a supermarket or the head olfice or an insurance company. You  
have ten minutes before the train leaves.  
Wbere Is the entrance?  
You have to search !or the entrance to the parking lot, and then  
you have to search !or the entrance of the building.  
*You have seven minutes ten.*  
The layout appears to be a labyrtnth.  
Once inside, you cannot figure out the layout. You don't understand  
bow the spaces relate to each other, nor can you Identify any  
underlying orgenlzalional principle. The cireulation sYBtem ,....  
mains a mystery to you.  
You cannot locale youraell In the station.  
You know you are somewhere in it, but where? Everything around  
you tends to look similar. For all you know, you may even have  
been at that same spot just a minute ago.  
Wbere are the elevaton?  
You try one corridor, then another.  
*Now you have !Ive mtnutes ten.*  
You may bave mlued a sign.  
You wonder if there was a sign or a map which could have been or  
help but which you, In your hurry, just overlooked.  
Thee you - **a** sign or a directory, but It Is bard to read.  
The lettering le too email, the contrast with the background poor. It  
is not well illuminated and the little light there le, comes bouncing  
back at you as if it had bit a mirror.  
There is too much information on the sign. It is chunked to-  
gether or scattered so that you can't read it. You see an electronic.  
cban8eable-mee""i'l sign but it moves too rapidly.  
*Three mt1Wlls ten.*  
You - **a** map but It la lfleelble.  
Looking at the map display, you get contused. It shows too many  
things and not what you tblnk you need. It somehow does not seem  
to represent the space you are In. It seems that It Is not aligned  
with the setting.  
*You have lost arwther valuable minute.*

1.2 **Who Is Inept at wayflndlng?**

*2.4* Safety

matter or life and death.

A setting Is safer II It ls wcU understood by the users and II they  
can easUy goet around In It. Should an exit be barred by fire or

that people have perished in lro11t ol conl!llsted emergency exits

3.1 Societal values  
*signs for* destinalions witbln too city and outside it. It includes  
reliable, consistently plsood *identification of* street names, places  
**of interest, civic and municipal offices, and so on.**  
'!be signage in Avignon is complemented by the design of the  
city streets, by the banning ol vehicular traffic from some of them,  
by the use or handsome bollards, to actually prevent cars lrom  
parking, rather than no-parking signs which would Utter the  
slreetscape, and by the absence ol parking meters, at least in the  
**North American sense.**  
Instead or parking meters, there is a machine, say every hun-  
dred meters, against the wall of a building \(slreets are narrow,  
sidewalks even narrower\), which dispenses parking tickets. Coins  
are inserted, a stamped ticket for a given period of time is issued.  
This is placed, lace up, on the motorist's dashboard for inspection  
by the police. The day wtll surely come when North American cities  
care enough to adopt Avignon's alternative to the parking meter.  
**Two anecdotes by Romedi Passini.**  
Digitized by **Google** 16  
A lew years ago an architect lriend. who was working with a large  
citizen group in Montreal. asked his audienoe what they considered  
to be the worsl building in the city. He hoped it would not be one of  
his but rather one of his competitors'. He was lucky, but totally  
amazed by the response or his audience. Everybody seemed to  
agree that the worst building in Montreal was a recent commercial  
center which had been published in respected architectural Jour-  
nals and which was generally held In high esteem by the proles-  
slon.  
This commerclal center was archltecturally quite appealing. It  
was constructed In a series of Interior terraces with a number of  
stairs coMectlng levels at dlllerent heigllts. It had many Interest-  
ing views and a contemporary architectural look to it. Compan?d to  
**other commercial centers, it certainly was of superior esthetie**  
quality.  
Why was it the worst building, my lriend asked in surprise?  
lkcause, they said, it is almost impossible to ftnd your way around  
in it. My lriend was stunned. He had never thought about way-  
fmding when designing *his* buildings either.  
This is not the end or the story. The center turned out not to be  
a commercial suocess. The shopping turnover never reached the  
planned levels and many people refused even to enter the building.  
In order to save the venture, the management decided to Invest  
In a massive "slgnage" project. Directional signs were carelully  
conceived and placed, maps or the settings were produced in  
various sizes. and even more, supergraphies and color coding were  
**used to identify the Doors and the major circulation pattern. No one**  
could quibble about the graphic design.  
In addition. the place looked even better. '!be redesign was  
lollowed by a publicity campaign, which had as leitmotiv\: "Come  
visit us, it's so easy to ftnd yqur way about." Did It work? No, it did  
**not.**

UNIVERSITY OF MICHIGAN . The various split-levels, the confusing spatial organization, the  
ambiguous circulation pattern, the repetitive architectural lea·  
**tures, the contradictory articulation of the interior and the exterior**  
- none of these factors could be solved by graphica. The problem  
was clearly arehitectural. The building was eventually lorn down.  
A press release mentioned that the building was found to be impos·  
sible for wayfmdJng purpcses.  
Here ill another example. During a reoonl visit to the University  
of Stuttgart, I was invited to give a lecture on wayfinding to the  
university's departmenl of planning. I was warned that I would  
probably not be able to nod my way, and took this as one of those  
jokes I had heard before. But it was not a Joke. I had to call for  
help.  
The uolverslty ls housed in a large, modernist complex with a  
great number ol entraoees. all looking about equally Important or.  
what ls closer to the truth, equally unimportant, and there Is no  
apparent logic to the layout.  
I bad been given a complicated eode, which described the  
address of my destination. but although I observed the use of  
colors on the !\~es. I stlll could make no sense of this informa-  
tion. Later. In discussion with my host, I was told a familiar story.  
After the bulldlngwas completed, It was discovered that students  
and stall were having dllflculties getting around, so it was decided  
to improve the signage.  
The graphic designer did his best. He tried to impose order  
with a color code where no architectural order was to be found. Hill  
**imposed system, however. was far from obvious and it became very**  
complex. Of course, it didn't do much good and he was naturally  
blamed for the fiasco. The fault, however, lay entirely with the  
architect.  
Architects, one would think, would be very keen to learn about  
wayfwding. Our experience up to now bas been to the contrary. It  
is stlll dilficult to get practising architects to Interest themselves in,  
or to read about, wayUndlng or to attract them *to* conferences. As  
we have already noted, it ls virtually Impossible to get wayflndlng  
courses Into the architectural education system.  
There must be many reasons for this apathy. Architects may  
feel, for example, that they already have too much to think about  
and do not want to be burdened with additlonal requirements.  
They may just be badly Informed, or the Information they have may  
not be presented In a form suitable for their use. Perhaps, however,  
it is *we* who are too impatient, perhaps we ought to allow more  
time for the message to get through.  
In this book we try to present not only individual recommends·  
lions but a wayfmding design approach. The internal coherence of  
this approach, we feel, will aciually make life easier for architects.  
We also hope to convince them that wayfwding is a key issue and  
not a peripheral one, and that wayfwdlng design is far from being  
an exclusively graphic design function.  
**A prime motive for writing this book has**  
**been the authors' keen desire to demon-**  
**strate that wayfinding, unlike s ignage. has**  
**more to it than graphic design - important**  
**as that may be. What used to be regarded,**  
**by architects, building owners. and graphic**  
**designers, as a purely graphic issue is now**  
**gradually coming to be seen as both an**  
**architectural and a graphk issue requiring**  
**close collaboration by the two disciplines.**  
**We are also beginning to see that this team**  
**\(architect and graphic designer\) will have to**  
**have at least a grasp of the fundamentals of**  
**environmental perception and cognition and**  
**of the principles of wayfinding.**  
**In addition. the team will need to recognize**  
**that wayfinding usually involves all of the**  
**senses - and not just one.**  
**Where are such people to be found7 And,**  
**more importantly, who will prepare them**  
**for this vocation? Who will do the teaching.**  
**and where?**

just typography and graphic design. Just as Important, II not more  
so, are the contributions of the architeets and transportation  
planners who laid out the concourses and other spaces that give  
**access to the mazes or tunnels and corridors, which work so well,**  
so seemingly effortlessly, in getting us to the right platform.  
**There is no end in sight to the manner in which "visual compo-**  
sition· will continue to enthrall designers for print. Nor, perhaps,  
should there be. although it would be pleasant, once in a while, to  
**come a.cross one whose professional interest in design extends to**  
**an interesl in content as well. It should. however, come to an**  
abrupt end when these same designers turn to signage.  
Unfortunately too many of us still think that the principles that  
have worked so well and been acclaimed in the area of print will be  
equally successful in that of signage. *AB* a result, we are now being  
Increasingly exposed to the robust, assertive, and vigorous use of  
such things as brass letters on stainless steel backgrounds or of  
wblte or charooa.l letlers on brushed \(or satin or, heaven presen·e  
us, mirror-finished\) aluminum.  
It will require a major change of mindset and of attitude on the  
part of graphic designers to wean themselves lrom these admit-  
tedly exciting "solutions· and to somehow convince them that true  
answers to wayfindlng problems Ue In other directions.  
**The graphic designer, previously considered**  
**as providing a "'service .. only, emerged in the**  
**80s as "'a strong Independent force on the**  
**printe-d page, invading it like a virus•;1**  
**•pages of design• are produced rather than**  
**•pages of text• and legibility is cheerfully**  
**sacrificed on the altar of this new.found**  
**independence. Should we be the least bit**  
**surprised, then, at the way in which this**  
**virus has infected the design of environmen·**  
**tal communkatlon devkes?**

Principles of wayfinding Chapter 4  
**What are spatial orientation and wayfinding?**  
In the literature on spatial behavior, wayflndlng la **a** new-  
comer. Introduced In the late 70s, It replaced the term "spatial  
orientation." Wayflndlng Is not Just a new and, we have to  
admit, now quite la.sblonable term for an old concept. It re-  
Oects a new approach to studying people's movements and  
their relatlonsblp to space. Even more Importantly, tbla new  
approach opens up new ways to design for people's spatial  
behavior.'  
In this chapter we sketch a brief research blatory and  
Introduce the basic concepts of spatial orientation, cognitive  
maps, and wayllndlng.  
Digitized by Google 22  
**Original from**  
UNIVERSITY OF MICHIGAN 4.1 Spatial orlentaUon\: the static relaUonshlp to space  
**The oldest mentions of spatial orientation, the forerunner of**  
wayfwding, appeared in neuropsycbological literature over a  
century ago. Neuropsychologists, such as Forster in t890, Meyer in  
1900, and Holmes in 1918, reported case studies of patients who, as  
victims of brain lesions, were incapable of even the most elemen·  
tary uoderstanding of where they were and of bow to reach given  
destinations in their everyday environment.•  
The deficiencies leading to these conditions of disorientation  
were later Identified as topographical or spatial agnosla and  
topographical or spatial anwesia. The key manifestation of spatial  
agnosla ls an lnablllty to l'l!COgnize spaces visited on previous  
occasions, wblle spatial anwesla leads to an lnablllty to link spaces  
mentally Into an overall representation.•  
Thls representation people have or their surrounding environ-  
ment, also called an Image or a cognitive map, ls the psychological  
concept that underlies the notion or spatial orientation. A cognitive  
map ls a mental construct or an environment which cannot be seen  
from one slngle vantage point alone. It bas to be composed from a  
series or Individual vistas. Cognitive mapping ls therefore a mental  
structuring process that Integrates into a whole what bas been  
perceived in parts.  
Brain lesions n!sultlng ln spatial or topographical amnesia, can  
destroy this cognitive mapping ability while leaving intact all other  
cognitive faculties. This ls a fascinating aspect of brain  
lesion research; it demonstrates the function of a particular ability,  
such as cognitive mapping, by showing what a person can and  
cannot do when deprived of that ability.  
The effect of spatial amnesia on people's ability to g\\'t around  
is dramatic indeed. At the beginning, amnesic patients are totally  
**incapacitated even if they find themselves in previously famUiar**  
environments. Aller a certain adaptation period, patients can learn  
to find their way by substituting verbal for the missing spatial  
ln!ormatlon. Rather than relying on a cognitive map ol a given  
route. they wUJ rely on a verbal checklist. A striking Ulustratlon ls  
given or a truck dri\\•er with spatial anwesla who, alter having  
learned to rely on cheekllsts Instead or physical maps, was able to  
get around sulOclently well to continue working ln his profession.•  
Depending on the spatial cbaracterlsties or an environment,  
cognitive mapping can be very dl!Ocult for everyone, and some-  
times it is an almost Impossibl e operation. Indeed, one or the main  
**disorienting characteristics of a labyrinth is precisely its inherent**  
di!Oculty to be understood in spatial terms and to be mentally  
**represented in the form of a cognitive map.**  
**Spatial orientation has been defined in various terms but all**  
**refer in one way or another to a person's ability to determine his or**  
**her location in a setting.**  
**From a cognitive perspective, spatial orientation is based on**  
the ability to form a cognitive map. You are considered spatially  
oriented U you have an adequate cognitive map of the surrounding  
selling and B1'l able to situate yourseU within that representation.  
This conceptualization of spatial orientation has not only

**representation of the spaces and the layout**  
**of a setting**  
**cognitive mapping\: the mental structuring**  
**process leading to the creation of a cogni-**  
**tive map**  
**spatial orientation\: the process of devising**  
**an adequate cognitive map of a setting**  
**along with the ability to situate oneself**  
**within that representation**

**W hat are spatial orientation and**

**4. t Spatial orientatton; the statJc relationship**  
**to spaoe**  
In *Th• Image of th\<\> C ity,* Lynch Identifies  
**five key environmental components people**  
**use to structure a representation of a city.**  
**He argues that visual accessibility and the**  
**prominence of five elements \(paths. land-**  
**marks, nodes, edges. and districts\) are the**  
**design criteria for highly legible and**  
**imageable city environments.**  
Digitized by Google  
generated a great deal of research on cognitive maps. tbelr nature,  
their composition, their evolution wben exploring a new setting, or  
their evolution throug\)I a life span, but it has also proven useful in  
exploring some or the spatial characteristi\<lS that f8'lUitate cogni-  
tive mapping. The credit for having made that all· important link  
between spatial orientation and planning goos without doubt to the  
pioneering work of Kevin Lynch.'  
Researchers working in the field of cognitive mapping were  
confronted by major methodological and conceptual problems. It  
proved extremely difficult to have people describe and externalize  
their spatial knowledge and to measure ii reliably. Furthermore, it  
was argued that the term "map" bad to be taken in a metaphorical  
**sense\: a cognitive map was not really a "'map .. as we know it from**  
geography or planning. It was maplike only in the sense that it  
**oonta.ined the same information as an actual map.**  
**6 The most**  
**critical issue oonoerned the notion of adequacy. which is crucial to**  
**the definition of spatial orientation. Researchers asked themselves**  
**if the criteria or an adequate oogn.iti,.-e map were its measurement**  
properties, Its topological properties. or other geometric character·  
!sties.  
The search for an answer v"as dampened by lhe observation  
tbal in many situations people got around quite well and dld not  
feel dlsorlented even If they bad only a very rudimentary under·  
standing or the setting.' Wustratlve examples could be found In  
anthropological Uterature describing navigation and palbfinding  
among Indigenous populations In homogeneous environments. sucb  
as sand deserts. oceans, and snow-covered expanses In the polar  
regions.  
Even way\(inders In contemporary urban settings often cannot  
rely on any topographical accuracy. Complex underground public  
transport interchanges. ror example, tend to be particularly dilfi·  
**cult to map, but people may not consider themselves disoriented as**  
**long as they know how to reach certain dcstinations.8**  
**It should be noted that even the most rudimentary cogniti\\•e**  
mapping distinguishes itself from spatial amnesia in that the  
person v .. ho is arniclcd by the latter is not capable or linking t\<\>-  
**gether tv.·o spatial experiences, even when one immediatel\)' follows**  
the other. The amnesic person can be compared to a reader \\vflo Is  
able to read and understand every word but cannot make the link  
with the previous words of a sentence and thererore cannot under-  
stand Its meaning.  
Even II the terms ·spatial orientation" and the underlying  
concept or "cognitive mapping" are perfectly suited to describe the  
static relationship ol a person to his or her spatial setting, they  
**cannot encompass the dynamic aspects of people's movement \(i.e.,**  
**the process involved in reaching chosen destinations\).** \~foreover .  
**these terms tend to imply disorientation in cases where people not**  
only are able to lind their way. but have developed very sopbisti·  
**cated navigational skills. for instance, traditional na\\•igation in**  
**Polynesia and hficronesia.9**  
Once Ibis ... 1lS understood In the late 70s, it became clear that a  
new concept was needed to account ror people's movement In  
sp.,,. and their sense of being 9f\~R W\~  
24 UNIVERSITY OF MICHIGAN \(.2 Wayftndlng\: the dynamic relaUonsblp to space  
"Wayfinding" was the term introduced to describe the process of  
**reaching a destination, whether in a familiar or unfamiliar environ·**  
ment. Wayfinding is best defined as spatial problem solving. •0  
Within this framework. wayfinding comprises three specific but  
interrelated processes\:  
• *Deci\$i\<m making* and the development of a plan of action  
• *Decisi\<m execution,* which transforms the plan into appropriate  
behavior at the right place in space  
• ***lnlurmation proce.'1si.ng* understood in its generic sense as**  
comprising environmental perception and cognition, which, in  
**turn. are responsible for the information basis of the two deci·**  
# sion·related processes  
At first, It may seem that the concept of a cognltlve map Is lost  
In this dellnlllon of wayfindlng. But this Is not so. Cognlllve map-  
ping is part of environmental perception and cognition. The cogni-  
**tive map is a source or inlonn.ation to make and execute decisions.**  
The relative importance of a cognltive map in the decision-making  
process depends on the nature of the setting and on the context of  
wayfmding. In other words, we have not eliminated the cognltive  
map, we have merely situated it within the much larll"r proooss of  
spatial problem solving.  
Wbat about disorientation? We argue that this is a personal,  
mental, and emotional state. People tend to feel disoriented when  
**they cannot situate themselves within a spatial representation and**  
when. at the same time, they do not ha'"" or cannot develop, a plan  
to roach their destination.  
**wayflnding\: spatial problem solving com·**  
**prising the following processes\:**  
• **Decision making**  
• **Oe-cision executing**  
• **Information processing**  
**Most settings are laid out in a plan \(or**  
**.. shape'"\) to which people can relate and**  
**which allows them to**  
• **determine their location within the**  
**setting,**  
• **determine that their destination is within**  
**the setting, and**  
• **form a plan of action that will take them**  
**from their location to their destination.**  
**When they are denied the ability to do any**  
**of these, they cannot form cognitive maps.**  
**They are effectively prevented from forming**  
**an efficient action plan. This. In turn. means**  
**that they must rely on other infOf'mation**  
**sources until they can. in fact. develop an**  
**appropriate plan. The undesirable alterna·**  
**tive is to let them wander about until. quite**  
**by chance, they stumble onto their objective.**

**How the wayfinding process works**  
Having Introduced the general Idea, we are now ready to study  
the clockwork or wt,yllndlng, that Is, to study what Is Involved  
when people attempt to reach spatial destinations.  
In tbls chapter, llrst we outline bow spatial problem solv-  
ing can be conceptualized, and then we describe In more

In studying decision plans on an empirical basis, we found that  
any complex wayf!odiog problem \(and most of them are complex lo  
the detailed analysis\) is broken down loto smaller problems whose  
solutions do not exceed three or four decisions. 7 Complex wayf!od-  
ing problems, thus, become more manageable; a particular aspect  
of the problem can be treated wllile stW keeptog In mind the prob-  
lem as a whole.  
This characteristic of planning is common to all probl em solv-  
ing.8 The planning of a building may serve as an illustrative exam-  
ple. The mnst general decision to plan it materializes in a contract.  
The flrst level of decisions may then correspond to the overall site  
planning, the following level to the layout of the building, and the  
last l e-•el to the working drawings.  
This type of problem solving leads to highly structured plans,  
wblch can also be easUy remembered. Indeed, structure is a major  
mnemonic aid. For most people it is impossible to relain even a  
small selection of random numbers. Some researchers bave even  
argued that magic is associated with the number seven because  
seven Items are the most we can remember lo an unstructured  
fashion. We believe that to remember even seven random units is  
asking a lot.  
On the other band, It is quite easy to retrace the steps of a  
route involving a great number of decisions. It ls also of Interest to  
note that in direct. ion giving, the csoacitv to describe a route In

**while lhe inquiring person receives a string of decisions in a un·**

Although we can recall many decisions ii they are structured.  
we can usually not recall them all. On the other hand, ii we find

likely r\<!Cl0gni7.e the place where we had to make the most detailed

presence ol the relevant situation\) Islar more elficlent than recall.  
**In the actual planning of a journey, a person**  
**may well start with a destination, let us say a**  
specific shop, which is located In a shopping  
**complex. which is in turn located downtown.**  
**When the plan is executed, the order will be**  
**inverted. The first destination is downtown,**  
**then the shopping complex, and then the**  
**specific shop.**  
**recognition\: \(re--cognizing\) remembering**  
**an item in the presence of the item**  
**recall\: remembering an item in the absence**  
**of the item**  
A plan ol action is a mental solution to a wayllndlng problem, but It  
does not In ltseU take you physically to your destination. Decisions  
must first be executed, that Is, they have to be translormed Into  
behavior. Most Importantly, each decision must be transrormed  
Into the right behavior at the right place.  
It Is not enough slmply to turn to the right, you wtll have to turn  
rlgbt at the appropriate Intersection. Decision executing Is, again,  
a hi\(!!!.ly complex proceas. This process has been well deserlbed In  
other publications. Let us here summarize some or the Important  
points relevant to wayflndlng design.'  
A wayfindlng decision is composed ol two parts\:  
I. Behavior. such as turning right, going up, or looking lor inlor-  
matlon  
2. An environmental entity, such as the Intersection, the stairs, or  
thebWboard  
**When executing a decision, we match a mental image or idea of**  
something specific in the environment with what we perceive in the  
environment. II we find the corresponding intersection, stair, or  
bUlboard, we execute the behavioral part ol the decision. If we  
cannot find the corresponding part in the environment, we have a  
very real problem. Because we cannot execute the decision, we  
have to develop a plan to solve this problem. This sllggesls bow to  
define a way!lndlng problem. A waylindlng problem ls a wayfindlng  
decision that cannot be directly executed and that tberelore re-  
quires lurther planning and decision maldng.

recognize them. Some patients bave been reported to recall lea-  
tures lrom the period preceding a trauma.

Even when taking very familiar routes, decision execution Is  
involved. This process. although highly complex, d\<\>es not require a

Taking a familiar route involves executing a recorded decision  
plan. That the reality is a bit more complex is again illustrated in  
neurological literature. Let us recall that people suffering from  
spatial amnesia due to brain lesions are not llble to map space  
cognitively. Although they can learn to get around with a verbal  
description of the necessary behavior, which is nothing other than  
a recorded decision plan, their waylinding efficiency and the ease  
by which Ibey can find their way is greatly reduced.  
In this recorded decision plan, they have access to all decisions  
and to all environmental reatures associated with these decisions,  
but they have no information about the spatial location or about  
what happens betwoon decisions. In other words, they would not be  
able to link one decision to the next *U* it were not lor their chrono-  
logical order.  
The cognitive map has the important support function ol situat-  
ing the decision in space. As we have seen, taking a lamlllar route  
is executing a recorded decision plan, but a plan that is linked to a  
spatial representation. Even if the representation is only partial  
\(and may even be rudimentary to the point of being almost non-  
**exis· tent\) in certain environmental contexts - even if it is limited to**  
**recognition on site rather than to recall - it still provides the**  
**wayfmder with a means of locating decisions in space or at least of**  
relating one decision to the next. The relationship and dynamics  
**bet¥ .. een decision plans and cognili\\•e maps are a most interesting**  
area of research.  
It bas been suggested by various authors that cognitive map-  
ping is supported by the formation and execution of travel plans."  
The hierarchical structure of decision plans helps us to remember  
not only decisions but also the spatial entities associated with  
them. This assumption explains the common experience that it is  
easier for people to remember a route if they make the wayfmding  
decisions by themselves than If Ibey are following a guide. Al-  
though when guided. they are exposed to the same environmental  
Information, because they are not making the decisions, but merely  
executing them. they are not effectl,.ely recording the decision  
plan.

5-3 lnlonnatloo processing  
Peroeptioo and cognltlon are the components or Information  
processing. Perception and cognition are interrelated, and it is  
often dilficult to distinguish one process clearly from the other. The  
**distinction is useJul. however\: one relates to the process of obtain·**  
ing information through the senses \(perception\), the other to the  
**understanding and manipulation of information, in particular**  
spatial information \(cognition\).  
Perceplion bas traditionally been a major field ol study in  
psychology. but. as William lttelson observed in 1973, we know  
**very little about environmental perception.1 2 This statement is still**  
true today. We know, in !act, very little about what people perceive  
in the everyday environment, bow they perceive while they are  
moving and being active, and bow they cope with complex situa-  
tions in which a lot or information bas to be processed.  
lttelson argued that the dillerenoe between the elementary  
peroeptlon or lorm, object. and depth, and environmental peroep-  
tlon was not just one or scale. Environments are peroelved through  
dllferent sensory modalllles. Environments are unbounded and  
surround a person, calllng !or exploration rather than passive  
exposure.  
Given their complexity. environments need to be peroelved  
seleellvely. and the relevance or the obtained lnlormallon needs to  
be assessed as well. Most Important or all, environments are per-  
ceived In the light of actMties. llnvlronmental peroeptlon ts. most  
of the time, directed and purposeful perception.  
In the following few pages we shall outline aspects or environ-  
**mental perception and cognition. Some come. from the professional**  
**literature while others have emerged from our studies of wayfind-**  
**ing. \\Vhenever possible Vle shall rerer to conceptual supports in the**  
field ol perceplion and cognition.  
**perception\: the process of obtaining inf or·**  
**mation through the senses**  
**cognition\: understanding and being able to**  
# manipulate information  
Scanning and glancing  
In reading these lines. if you are a patient reader, you will system-  
alically move your eyes from left to rigbt and from the top to the  
bottom or the page. If you are a speed reader you will have fewer  
points of rlX&tion but still you will go through the page in a very  
**similar pattern. The environment, hov-·ever, is not read like a book.**  
**Environmental perception is based on a process of scanning and**  
glancing.  
When moving through a complex selling, the eye scans the  
visual field. Tbis pre-allentive perception serves to identify objects  
or messages or interest." These obje\<-'ls or messages are then  
focused upon !or a short period of time. During this short locus or  
glance, the eyes rest on the item of interest for some tenths or a  
second." Tbe lmage thus obtained is held in a short-term visual  
memory untU It ls translated into memory or longer duration.

**Complexit;y vs..**

Complexity **and** overload  
One or the environment's most striking characteristics ls tu. com-  
plexity. The wayllndlng person cannot take in everytltltlg, but must  
select that which Is useful, in other words, that which ls relevant to  
the decision-making process.  
We have been able to document many instances which show  
that when people are executing a decision plan or part or a plan,  
they will perooive Information directly relevant to that plan. Infor-  
mation that is not directly applicable to the immediate plan, even if  
**it would be relevant sometime later, tends not to be seen. The.**  
**location of information is therefore a crucial issuc,. It is not an**  
exaggeration to say that information at the wrong place is as good  
**as no information at all**  
**In certain complex settings, thls selection of information could**  
be more difficult. This is particularly true if wayllnders are bom-  
barded with stimulation ol all sorts and if, at the same time, they  
**must plow through this excess to fmd relevant information. In such**  
cases, a condition of overload could develop in which people reduce  
their intake or information as an ultimate coping device. The result  
is that even if they are looking at the relevant information, they are  
not able to process it.  
It should be noted that a lot ol stimulatlon does not in itself  
necessarily lead to overload. The environment always contains  
more information than we can process. Overload O\<X\>Urs when  
stimulation interferes with purposeful Information processing. It ls  
only when a person ls aetl,,ely looking !or Information in a contus-  
ing environmental context that the risk or overload O\<X\>Urs. Over-  
load can be averted by design interventions aimed at helping the  
user to find the relevant information.

**There are three different types of braille.**  
**Grade 1 braille is strict transliteration of the**  
**original language; however. grade 2 braille,**  
**which includes many contractions, is much**  
**more effie\:ient to use. Grade 3 is a type of**  
**shorthand and does not concern us here.**  
**Onty about one in ten of those registered**  
**with blind organizations can read grade 2**  
**braille, although rather more can read grade**  
**1\. The assumption that every blind person**  
**can read braille is certainly false.**

• PcrceMng an original sound source in the environment  
• PerceMng sound produced by the person \(e.g., cane lapping\)  
and reflected by objects in tbe environment \(ech\<\>-looatlon\)  
The value of a sound source for wayfindlng Is often reduced  
because of the unreliability of tbe source. You can, for example, see  
a tree from a given distance, but you can only bear It wben the  
wind blows. Ech\<\>-location is more reliable in this sense, but It  
**requires a relatively quiet environment and only works at small**  
distances. You can "bear" a tree through ech\<\>-location only if you  
are direcUy in Iron! of it.  
II bas been argued that only blind people have developed the  
ability to use sound to identify objects. We don't think this is so. In  
an unpublished study done a few years ago \(Romedi Passini\),  
blindfolded, sighted subjects were also found, surprisingly. to be  
capable of ldentlfy!ng openings, barriers, and even relatively smali  
overhangs through ech\<\>-looation.  
Sounds are perceived regardless ol bead position. Sounds for  
that reason are excellent warning cues. Crossing a busy slreet  
without being able to bear is quite an unsetUing experience for  
anybody. Indeed, deaf people, because they cannot rely on sound  
signals, tend to be excessively !earful of accidents. To make lhings  
worse, almost ali evacuation warnJng systems \(flre alarms, for  
example\) are based on sound signals. Salety Is probably one of the  
ma jor conceros or deaf and hearing-Impaired people.  
Sound, by the same token. ls much more dllficult to screen than  
visual stimulation. Screening, when using a hearing aid, ls particu-  
larly troublesome. This in itself is a good reason why we should be  
concerned about the soundseape or our built environment. We are  
singularly indifferent to noise pollution -even when we know that  
**noise obscures informative sounds and that the effects of noise. on**  
stress and health have been clearly demonstrated.  
Tactual and baptie perception apply only to proximal objects.  
**The white cane of the blind person is an extension of his or her**  
**a.rm. but that is as far as it goos. The cane sen-es to identify obsta·**  
cles and to follow direction-giving lines such as a curb or the edge  
or a footpath.  
**Braille and raised letters, raised numbers, and even some**  
raised symbols are read by the blind population. The difficulty with  
using tactile slgnage, however, ls that blind way!inders fll'St must  
know where the information Is located before they are able to use  
it. Furthermore. it has been noted that a social stigma ls attached  
to tactile exploration. At a conference held by the SOOD \(Society of  
Environmental Graphic Designers\) in 1988, a blind speaker,  
Michele Brule, illustrated this point with the poignant question\:  
"Do you see me exploring the white wali or the Impeccable glass  
door or the corporate omee in order to find the company's name?"  
Tactile maps ha"e been shown to be use ful to blind travellers.  
Just like the reading of braille and of raised letters and symbols,  
the reading of tactile maps has to be learned. !Wsearchers have  
**identified characteristics of tactile maps that facilitate their use.**  
**They have also developed techniques £or producing tactile maps**

Few would argue that we have lost the ablllty to lollow our  
noses, although our sense or smell ls not usually enough to get us  
home. Our pereeption or odors Is simply not fine enough to give us  
many directional cues. Our sense or smell may have some limited  
use aa a cue for place Identification but It tends to be very unrell-  
able In our contemporary environment.  
Some research baa shown that stimulations, usually associated  
with one particular sense, could be perceived by other senses as  
well. A French psychologist by the name of Duplessis, tried to show  
that discrimination of colors without sight was possible. According  
to her studies, about 20 percent of the population is able to dis-  
criminate colors by what she calls dermo-optic perception. We  
tried to replicate the experiments In a wcll-rontrolled study, but  
**\\\\'ere not able to produce any similar fmdings.1 8**

The meaning or cognilion is knowing and understanding. Two  
aspects or environmental cognition can be dlstlnguished\:

whlch has already been described as a cognitive map  
The things we remember  
It may be or interest to know which buildings people remember  
best, but more important for the planner ls an understanding or the  
physical and non-physical characteristics that make buildings  
memorable. Researchers studied these characteristics and arrived  
at four !actors that enhance memory for buildings In a cityscape\:  
t. The rorm or the building; us size, contours, compleXity or shape.  
and uniqueness or architectural style  
2. Vlslblllty and access\: pedestrian access and the posslblllty or  
moving around the building  
3. Use; II a building had an Important !unction and was used orten,  
It was also well remembered  
4. Symbolic slgnlllcance\: elderly citizens were particularly aware  
or historical and cultural meanings associated with buildings"  
Cognitive mapping

types of cognitive mapping\:  
l. People may structure the environment in terms or routes, that is,  
the points where they change direction, the angle or directional  
**change, and a measure of tne distance from one point to ana**  
**other.**

UNIVERSITY OF MICHIGAN Part Three\:  
Principles of wayfinding design Chapter 6  
**What is wayfinding design?**  
Wayfindlng requirements, whether they be at the regional,  
urban, or architectural scale, are Integral to tbe design pro-  
cess - from tbe most general, overall spatial organization of  
the setting to the articulation of tbe form-giving festures, and  
right down to the Individual architectural and graphic mes-  
sages. Wayftndlng requirements shape the setting, allect  
the choice of the circulation system, and contribute to the  
design of tbe Interior. Tbls ls particularly true In large build-  
ing complexes.  
The enormous Impact ol wayflndlng cooslderatloos on the  
outcome of the design of a setting Is one of the reasons we feel  
justtned In using tbe term "wayflnd.lng design."  
The other reason Is strategic. Wayflndlng Is a very Impor-  
tant aspect of dally We and bas been neglected for so long that  
we want to give It Its rightful place on tbe drawing board. We  
are totally convinced on this subject. Even II It can be argued  
that, In tbe past, the built enVlronment was relatively simple  
and clear, this Is certainly not true of today's mammoth build-  
ing complexes.  
When "wayllndlog design" becomes a consideration In Its  
own right \(llke "HVAC" or •emergency exit procedures"\),  
planoers wW be made aware that It Is a vital part of design,  
and II they pride themselves on being professionals, they  
should know about It. Wayllndlng design Is also a principle.  
It Is user oriented and derives Its approach and Its Interven-  
tions from the behavioral and psychological fonndatlons of  
wayllndlng.  
l!lllclent, accessible, sale, and spatially attractive wayflnd-  
lng environments can be designed only when the principles  
of wayflndlng are understood and when this knowledge Is  
translated Into applicable form. Tbe aim of this chapter ts  
to make the link between design and the wayflndlng princi-  
ples Introduced previously. Two aspects of wayflndlng design  
wW retain our attention\: spatial planning and environmental  
communication.

UNIVERSITY OF MICHIGAN 6.1 Spatial planning  
Wayflndlng occurs Jn space. The spatial cba.racterlstlcs or a site,  
that ls, Its size, Its organization, and the nature or Its elreulatlon  
systems, all contribute In one way or another to the way!lndlng  
dllficultles that confront the site's users.  
Spatial planning provides the context !or wayflndlng and sets  
the stage !or the problem-soMng per!ormance. Spatial planning  
determines the location of the entrances and exits or a setting, the  
location of the major destinations and, there!ore. the nature of Its  
circulatlon system, the organization of Its spaces, and the visual  
accessibility of Its architecture. Spatial planning not only defines  
the wayfmdlng problems or the future users. but affects the ease or  
difficulty they wUI have in understanding and cognitively mapping  
the setting. Spatial planning is, therefore. the first major compo-  
nent in wayfmding design.

The content and location of Information  
Let us first look at the decision-making and decision-executing  
aspects of wayfinding.  
***U,* as we ba,·e outlined above, information is needed for each**  
wayfinding decision that is made and executed, it follows lhat lhe  
particular lolormation that the wayfinder gathers is determined by  
lhe decisions that lhat person must make.  
It is lhe decisions or. when seen lo their totality, the decision  
plan that is the determlnlog factor lo designing an lolormatlon

lheo lhe lolormatlon has to be provided through architectural.  
graphic, or other. non-graphic means.  
It is important to emphasize that waylloding design should be  
concerned not only with lndlvidual decisions but with a series of  
them \(i.e., the structured ensemble ol decisions that leads the  
wayfindlng person to his or her destination\).  
The logic of the decision plan \(or dlagram\) is the rationale that  
**tics individual decisions into a whole. and this same rationale must**  
# tie the information units into the overall system.  
Much signage is concerned only with installing signs al inter-  
**sections. Such a procedure ignores the information needed for**  
decisions lhat do not directly lead to behavior. In other words, it  
ignores lhe higher-order decisions structuring the plan.  
*An* lolormalion unit has to be perceived at, or shortly before. a  
decision point. Otherwise the lolormation tends not to be noticed.

designer has to transfer the d.ctsions ol the diagram to the route.

Some practical considerations  
The reader at this point may well wonder if our approach. although  
plausible in principle, is at all practical. It might be thought, for  
example, that in a complex setting there are as many dlf!ercnt  
**decision plans as there. arc routes, and that Cor each route there**  
**are as many decision plans as there are people. IC this were really**  
**so, however, v.•e would have to forget about wayfinding design**  
altogether.  
**It is true that in a.ny large setting an almost infmite number of**  
way!inding tasks and routes can be identified - if one were to link  
all possible points ol origin lo all possible points or destination. It is  
**also true that some routes would be more representati,·e of peo-.**  
pie's movement in the setting \(i.e., primary routes\) and that others  
\(I.e., secondary and tertiary routes\) would be of lesser importance.  
Elsewhere we have described three kinds of primary routes\:  
• **The main circulation between the entrances or exits or a setting**  
**and the major destination zones**  
• **The circulation from one major destination zone to another**  
• **The circulation within a major destination zone**  
**Original from**  
UNIVERSITY OF MICHIGAN The nature and size of the major destination zones wlU vary  
!rom site to site. E\:xamples of major destination zones for large  
multipurpose urban or suburban complexes may be the omce  
section, the residential section, or the shopping section. The prl·  
mary routes \(even of very complex settings\) are quite limited in  
numbers. II the information system is clear and effective for the  
primary routes, it will set the waylinding tone for the whole of the  
setting. The system will dominate the secondary and tertiary  
routes. Specific information will still be needed for these latter  
routes, but they will be integrated into the overall scheme.  
Do people who take the same route develop different decision  
plans? This question is crucial. II everybody were to develop !unda·  
**mentally different decision plans, these plans would be of no use in**  
the design of wayfinding information systems. Fortunately, how·  
ever, there is a direct link between the spatial organization of a  
set Ung with its related circulation system and the most appropri·  
ate decision plan.  
Each type of circulation demands its own type of decision plan.  
Thus, ii the appropriate Information is provided, people on the  
whole will develop slmllar plans. This makes good sense. Alter all,  
tbe waylinding problem is the same for all people, and ii the Infor-  
mation to solve the problem Is the same, the solution should be the  
same as well. This does not, however, exclude people's having  
preferences and choosing one route rather than another for a  
multitude of personal reasons.  
Empirical wayfindlng data shows this to be essentially true, but  
it also points to the need for further clarification.  
The information a person seeks in order to solve his or her  
wayfmding problem is not determined by the environmental setting  
alone. It also depends on bis or her preference for a certain kind of  
**information - whether it is linear and sequential \(such as one**  
might find on signs\), or whether it is spatial and global \(such as  
that emanating !rom the spatial organization of the setting itself\).  
These two way!inding styles are not exclusive. Most people use  
**both. They will at various times have preferences for one over the**  
other. These preferences are adjusted according to the character-  
istics of the setting or to wayfinding conditions \(i.e., the normal,  
·everyday," the recreational, and the emergency wayf10ding condi·  
lions. which are described at length in Chapter 9\). The reader may  
have already made the Unk between these information preferences  
and the typology or cognitive maps discussed in the previous  
chapter.  
People who have a tendency to structure information on a  
sequential basis will also have a tendency to seek this type of  
information. On the other hand, people who structure information  
on a spatial basis will seek information giving them an idea of bow  
the setting is organized, and how it is linked by the circulation  
system.  
It should be noted that while the decision plans in both eases  
**may be identical, the information leading to the decisions m&\:y**  
vary. It is therefore important that both spatial and linear informa·  
lion be provided to allow for both way!inding styles.

**6.2 Environmental communication**  
Up to this point we have concentrated on the content and the loca-  
tion of wayflnding information. The third major aspect of environ-  
mental communication Is *f\<\>nn.* which Is concerned not only with  
esthetics but also with key information processing aspeets. Envi-  
ronmental perception and cognition provide the theoretical founda-  
**tions for the formal aspect of environmental communication.**  
**These matters are discussed again in graphic**  
**terms in Chapter 13.**  
**legibility\: the ease with which information**  
**is able to be perceived**  
**reJKlabllfty\: the ease with which** informa\~  
**tion is able to be understood**  
Legtblllty and nwl•blllty  
Typically, visitors trying to make use of the information displayed  
**for their benefit may encounter one of h\\.·o major flaws\:**  
• The Information may not be legible in that It Is obstructed. badly  
pl4'l00, too small, blurred, garbled, or tactually too mushy to be  
perceived.  
• The information may not be readable in that It can be perceived  
but cannot be understood.  
Many people refer to the terms "legibility• and "readability• as  
though they were Jntercbangeable. They aren't.  
Leg!blllty ls a key Issue and needs speelal mention In this  
chapter on design principles. In the previous secllon, we saw that  
environmental perception ls based on a process of scanning and  
glanelng. Objects or mess\~ are focused upon for a short period  
of time. The Image thus obtained Is held in a short-term visual  
\(iconic\) memory which has a llmlted retention capacity.  
The llmited capaci!\)' of this short-term visual memory bas a  
marked impact on the graphics, in particular on the presentation of  
written mes\~. Studies have shown that on signs and maps only  
**a small number of written items. generally throe. at most. can be**  
read at a glance. If more than throe items are presented. they  
should be grouped into packages not exceeding that desired *limit.*  
The wayfindlng person will then be able to read the m essage in a  
few glances \(see also section 13.4.1\).  
Most written information displays, such as signs and maps, arc  
not designed with this basic perceptual rule in mind. They arc hard  
lo read in a glance, and the users. ii they think that they need tbe  
information badly enough. have to stop and resort to a more linear  
"book reading" style.  
Legibility and readability In complex settings can also be  
affected by a state of mind often described as general confusion,  
whlcb ls brought about by information overload. This phenomenon  
of Information processing can be averted by design Interventions  
that help the user to find the relevant information.  
Consistency in the design of Information displays will help a  
wayfindingperson's search by lndlcatingwhat to look for. The  
form that the information takes, the material used. and the graph-  
**ics arc all contributing elements. An information display should be**  
able to be identified before it can actually be read. People learn to  
**recognize such displays after having seen them just onoo or twice**  
before.

Environmental communication is a blanket term the.I embraces  
architectural information as well as graphic and verbal \(or audi-

design is a relatively new calling, dating only from the early 606.  
Until recently, it recruited its members exclusively from the ranks  
of graphic designers.  
In the early 70s the Society of Environmental Grapblc Design-  
ers \(SEGO\) was formed with offices in Cambridge, Massachusetts,  
and ever since has assumed a leading and inOuential role In rais-  
*ing* professional standards and in broadening an understanding of  
the graphic designer's function in the environmental and architec-  
tural contexts.  
The SEGO bas also taken a lively and intelligllnt interest in  
**education - so far. hov.rever. in the race or a less-tban·li\\•ely re-**

ta! communication.  
Tbls ralses a problem In that Increasingly in future, arcbltects

very few Institutions are Interested In preparing them for tbls.

scribed in the preceding section. In any case, the role ol the arcbl-  
tect has always been well established and we aU know wbat archi-  
tects do. Whal. however, does the graphic designer do In tbls new  
**context of collaboration on environmental communications**  
projects? His or her prime contribution is a thorough knowledge of  
the effective planning and organization of tw\<Hlimensional spaee.  
This knowledge must be tempered by a more than nodding  
acquaintance with industrial design and the requirements of pubUc  
safety.  
In addition, it must be assumed the graphic designer on the  
team is thoroughly up to date on developments in the sign industry.  
which, with the advent or computeNiriven equipment \(such as  
computerized cutters and plotters\), have been advancing very  
rapidly.  
The grapblc designer also bring\$ an extensive knowledge that  
Includes the following grapble tecbnlques\:  
• hand graphics  
• typograpblcs  
• pictograpblcs  
• photographies

generally to be expected from the graphic designer, knowing about  
**the processes of environmental perception and cognition is more**

wayllnding design and, therefore, needs to be considered a part of  
the graphic designer's education.  
**Original from**  
UNIVERSITY OF MICHIGAN **Finally, because environmental communication is not limited to**  
arcbltectural and grapblc information, but comprises audible or  
**verbal information as well, some member of the team must be**  
conscious of the important role that voice will play in the context of  
wayfinding design.  
**We now know for certain that some forms of information within**  
the environment are most effectively communicated through sound.  
People would, for example, much rather \(ii they had the choice\) be  
*told* bow to get to their destination than have to figure it out from a

It therefore becomes Incumbent on the team to provide for

function to provide guidelines for tra!nlng the personnel who wUJ  
staff the Information desks.\)  
All of this ls a far cry from the role the graphlc designer Is

Carpenter \(writing about waylindlng in *Print* magazine\) put It 80

Landscape archltects are Involved In waylindlng design when  
planning large areas for recreation and work sucb as parks. golf  
courses, athletic facilities, promenades, and bicycle paths.  
Wayfmding design in archltecture and in landscape archltecture  
have many aspects in common. In planning the layout, they set the  
wayfmding stage; in articulating the landscape, they communicate  
the necessary information to the waylinding person.  
In terms of waylinding design, landscape differs from arcblte\<\>  
lure not 80 much in content as in the vocabulary used. Landscape  
arcbltects also compose with paths, landmarks, and destination  
**zones. They also organize space and communicate a circulation**  
system.  
One of the underestimated functions of landscape architecture  
Is site planning. In the conception of a campus, a large residential  
area. or a large exhibition complex, It Is landscape architecture  
that defines the spatial organization and the circulation system at  
the extra-building scale.  
If we focus on the bulldlng Itself, the landscape can put Its form  
Into perspective. It can make the bull ding visually accessible and  
can emphasize the location of Its entrances. In certain cases,  
where the doors are hidden, It may well be that the landscape  
provides the only available information about where to enter.  
Special features of the landscape can help users to distinguish  
one part of the setting from another and thus assist the cognitive-  
mapping process. Landscape features can even announce the  
spatial organization the visitor will find inside the building. V1Stas

Blind travellers have to rely on auditory and tactlle cues. Only

This in itself reduces the compensatory value of sound perception

Even with its shortcomings, however, auditory perception is  
most Important to the mobile blind person and it has to be  
opllmlzed as mucb as possible. Background noise has to be coo·

encountered at the entrances of underground subway systems.

service of personnel trained to guide blind travelers to their desti·

bonus.

liS Peroeptual lmpelrment  
To give verbal wayflndillg information to a blind person is not  
as easy as it might sound. II has boon our experience tbat it works  
much better the other way round. Blind people usually remember  
street names, and their descriptions of places arc often much  
clearer and more precise than are tbe descriptions given by the  
sighted. Quite apart from using visual cues, the sighted population  
puts less effort into understanding settings in order to get around,  
and this affec!B information giving' It is therefore suggested tbat  
training for people working in information booths include wayflnd-  
ing communication to the blind.  
The energy and attention required from a mobile blind traveler  
is enormous. Many blind people, when reaching middle age, feel  
drained of energy and exhausted by the tasks of wayfinding in  
**unfamiliar as well as in familiar environments. Even just rrom a**  
humane point of view, architects and graphic designers ought to  
take into consideration the needs of tbis population, both to facili-  
tate mobility and to reduce tbe risks or accidents.  
"Visual residue· Is a generic term that covers various visual  
handicaps. Individuals may have a low acuity of tbe whole visual  
field, such as blurred vision due to cataracts. They may peroelve  
only part or the visual neld. Some may experience "night blind-  
ness" In poorly illuminated settings. Some may be temporarily  
blinded lrom strong Ugh! contrasts.  
Certain design criteria can facilltate the perception of relevant  
wayflndillg information for the visually impaired, among the most  
important\:  
• The use or strong color and brightness contrasts In signage  
• The avoidance of contrasts as well as of glare In the general  
level or illumination  
• Visual and tactlle dennitlons or main circulation routes to set  
them apart from open areas  
• The pairing or visual with non-visual sensory lnlormatlon  
These criteria for lacilllating visual perception will also benefit the  
population at large and the elderly in particular.  
Up to this point, it could be argued that if a setting is well  
designed for tbe population in general, it will also work reasonably  
well for tbe visually impaired and blind population. We say ·rea-  
sonably well" because there are still some minor Interventions that  
will greatly benefit the blind population. Blind people, for example,  
have d.Ullcultles traversing large open spaces without some form of  
directional guide. They need warning signals In order not to coillde  
with obstacles or fall Into openings. These are, however, minor  
design lntel'\\'entlons and do not need to affect the overall design  
approach.

**Original l rom**  
66 UNIVERSITY OF MICHIGAN **and transformers. for example, cause static interference, which**

A deaf person, in order to communicate, bas to rely on written

Some o! the deaf mlgbt have dlfOeulties understanding the

One of the major problems common to all handicapped groups,

ashamed o! their handicap or find the emotional effort to get infor-  
mation too great for the reward. A concerted effort on the design  
and educational sides will probably lead to a reduction in the  
problem. Confidence is built up over time by the feedback o! re-  
warding experiences. Wayflnding design can contribute to the  
reinforcing effect and thus affect the well-being o! everyone.

Development refers to the whole life span. Its impairment may

**Dementia caused by Alzheimer's disease and related disorders**  
affect an important section of the elderly population. It is estimated  
that approximately I percent of North Americans sutler lrom de-  
mentia wb.ich is considered to be the fourth leading cause of death.  
Alzheimer's ls an Irreversible degenerative disease of the brain  
which dlsables various cognitive functions at dlllerent rates.• Some  
of its earllest manllestatlons are contusion, memory loss, and dis-  
orientation with respect to tlme and space. Alzbelmer's patients  
tend to forget recent 0\\'llnta wbUe keeping some or their longterm  
memory. They tend to wander and easily get lost. Wandering is  
particular to Alzheimer's. Gllleard \(1984\) identified throe causes\:  
8Altivity seeking, habitual walking behavior, and disorientation.  
What is the cause of disorientation? Some researchers explain  
it as a general loss of memory and cognitive abilities; others as-  
sume that the degeneration ls selectlve and that the spatial abili-  
ties are particularly prone.  
Among the spatio-cognitive abilities, It Is those requiring  
higher-order representations that are struck fll'SI. Alzheimer's  
patients may still be able to operate efficiently, although at a more  
**elementary level It is also assumed that Alzheimer's patients are**  
not able to process bigll levels of information. II they do operate at  
the limits of their capacities. they can experience stress and over-  
load.  
The therapeutic objective In Institutions, on the other band, Is  
to support the bighest 10\\ • el or functional utility In order to rein-  
force competence and sell esteem. These considerations point to  
an optimal middle ground in which the environment is stimulating  
**without being overbearing. Alzheimer's patients seem to require a**  
**specialized environment. which. as we have indicated, can have**  
therapeutic !unctions. For example, rather then trying to eliminate  
wandering, lbe provision of sale and well.\<felined wandering paths  
has been suggl!flled.'

8.5 ll!!teracy  
Many more people are runct!onally illiterate tban one would like to  
think. Hard statlstlcs based on actual ability are virtually impossi-  
ble to obtain because !Wteracy, in our society, ls generally consld·  
ered a shameful subject.  
11 we describe a person who cannot read and write sufficiently  
well to fill a job application as illiterate, we may estimate the  
illiteracy rate at 20 percent. Typographic signs are by no means  
completely useless to this group. Many of them are able to read a  
Utt!e, or at least to recognize certain key words from tbeir shape or  
their llrst letter.  
We all are !Wlerate In some ways and In some situations. II we  
expand the meaning or !Wteracy to Include an Inability to read a  
written messagll in a given language, we have to Include In tbe  
illiterate group anybody visiting a foreign country who does not  
speak its language and we also have to include clllldreo or pre-  
school age.  
Symbols and pictographs are the obvious alternative to written  
messages. They have demonstrated tbeir usefulness in coping with  
International visitors In settings such as world fairs, airports, and  
public transport terminals - provided tbeir content is limited to  
very simple concepts such as washrooms, phones. or baggage  
# claims.  
Pictorial languagll. like any other. bas to be based on a set or  
recognized elements, and this implies a certain level of standardl-  
zatlon or content. For years, European railway stations have used  
the same sort of pictographs to identi/y baggage claims, money  
exch\~. waiting rooms, and otber faellities. These signs are now  
well understood by everyone and they are as effective as verbal  
descriptions.  
Symbols and pictographs also have their shortcomlngs, how-  
0\\'cr. There are limits on what can be described or ldentll!ed In a  
pictograph and tbere is also a limit to the number of dlflereot  
pictographs tbat can be used In a sign. This issue is discussed In  
rurther detail In Chapter 13.  
As observed earlier. we are all likely to find ourselves functionally  
!Wterate at one tlme or another. Visiting a loreign country is one  
of them; living In an ofllclally bilingual country like Canada is

8.7 Macro- **and** micro-approaches to planning  
• AcccBS to lnlormatlon" ls what It's all about and, In the Ugtit of the  
new-found Interest In, and concern for, the problems of the disa-  
bled, the quoatlon arises\: How do we go about planning environ-  
mental communications In tho first plaoo?  
Selwyn Goldsmith distinguishes two dlllerent design ap-  
proaches.  
'' Tho ·macro-approach· sets a bJgb standard within  
which the disabled are treated as ·normal.· Their needs, In terms  
of physical requirements, are accounted for as & matter of course.  
Tbe usual provisions for the whole population become accommo-  
dating lor them **as** .... u . The macro-approacb trtes to make all  
buildings """""81ble to all l\)'pe8 of users.  
In the "mlcro-approaeb, • h""1!\\-el', the disabled person la  
treated **as a** special case with specific requirements which can be  
accommodated 0111¥ by provisions made espe\<'ially for them. Tbla  
approach leeda to the creation of selected settings. speclllcally  
designed for the ''ery special needs of the handicapped.  
DlecuBSlone about tile wayllndlng requirements of the disabled  
population tend to favor the macro-approaeb for tile design of  
pubUc buUdlnjl9. It la only lor very severe cognltlve dlsabllille&,  
such as Ahhclmcr'a disease, that a specialized environment bo-  
comcs necessary. To plan for tbe population as a whole helps the  
gllncral public and cspeclally the majority or elderly persons who  
may have several different \~cnts.

**For whom do we plan?**  
8.7 M8"ro- and mk\:ro-appro60h .. to pl&nnlng  
'Ibe macro-approacb assures a blgber overall standard or  
planning; moreover, it does not create artificial barriers between  
populalion groups and does not ostracize the handicapped.  
'Ibe design ol accessible and sale settlng8 is now a political  
issue. It is only tbanks to pressure groups, spearheaded by wheel-  
chair users In the early 70.., that the population as a whole and  
politicians In particular have been made aware ol the issue.  
From the political point or view, the macro-approach would  
seem to be more acceptable than the micro. It Is always easier to  
ask !or a provision that benefits the populalion as a whole than to  
ask !or one that benefits only a speclllc group because. even II that  
group bas every right to make such a NOquest, it tends to be pe"  
ce!ved as a special lavor.  
The macro-approach to planning does not preclude small  
adjustments when they are needed. Althougb planning standards  
should be based on the N!QUirements ol the population as a wboie,  
**circumstances may require that minor interventions be initiated**  
!or a given disabled population.  
In line with our own support !or tbe macro-approach, the  
authors have chosen not to write a special chapter on "way!lnding  
and the disabled population.• Rather, we Incorporate references to  
their normal and some or their specillc N!Qulrements In the body or  
the text.

# 9.2 Emergency oondltlons  
**It is commonly assumed that in serious emergency situations**  
people wiU panic. Jonathan Sime, who bas done extensive researeb  
on peoples' behavior in fire. bas determined that this assumption Is  
basically wrong.  
Investigating the behavior Of people who bad SW'\\1Ved fires,  
Sime round that they wnded to behave In a controlled and rational  
way - as long as they had a hope or getting out and being saved.  
**The rationale ror their behavior was not necessarily that or the**  
planning arebitect or the lire marshal. but of people who bad to  
deal with w hatever information they bad about the building. It is  
no\~ therefore. a sign of irrationality that they wnd lo move along  
previously experienced routes and to i8J!ore nre exits.'  
Any rire marshal wiU confirm the fact that the sln8!e most  
Important !actor in saving people's lives In a fire Is time. Through  
the production or combustible \(and toxic\) gases, fires expand lo  
leaps and bounds. Seconds may make all the dilference between  
life and death. Therefore. to delay the announcement or a fire in a  
public building for fear that people wiU panic is not only ill advised.  
but irresponsible.  
Panic is not usually part of wayfmding behavior in eme'll"n·  
**cies. However, streSB is, and stress does affect performance in one**  
ol two ways. Depending on the context and the level ol stress.  
stress can either reduce or improve performance.'  
II stress leads to a b1gb level of anxiety, the ellect always tends  
to be negative.• Worrying also consumes energy. Psychological and  
cognitive resourees have to be allocated to deal with anxiety. and  
**these resources are therefore. not available for wayfmding.**  
**Furthermore, information that is processed to control anxiety**  
**may compete with the relevant wayfinding information, thus creat-**  
**ing a condition similar to that of overload. Thus, e'°·en if informa-**  
tion were available. a person migbt not be able to absorb it.  
Consistency lo the form and location ol environmental communica-  
tion is the best way to reduce overload.  
Stress can be caused by the pereepllon or danger \(for example  
the sight or smoke\), by time constraints, and by uncertainty. The  
**danger may be real and the time limitations important.**  
**The only cause of stress that can be reduced is uncertainty.**  
Uncertainty about the person's safety is reduced by keeping people  
informed of the nature and location of the danger. Uncertainty  
about escape can be reduced by providing the necessary  
wayfinding Information. Information ls the best means of lessening  
stress.  
The overall knowledge people ha"e about the spatial character-  
istics or the setting with its entrances and exits will probably be  
**their most valuable information. If the setting is well understood by**  
**the users - that is, if they have a clear cognitive map, they will not**  
**only have all the decision-making information at their disposal,**  
**Original from**  
80 UNIVERSITY OF MICHIGAN they will also be able to develop alternative options should a par-  
tleular exit turn out to be barred by hazards.
