UNIVERSITY OF MICHIGAN 10.1 The process of planning a layout  
Seen lrom a wayfinding perspective, three phases or spatial plan-  
ning stand out\:  
• Identification ol the consliluent spatial units  
• **Grouping of spatial units into destination zones**  
• Organization and linkage ol units and zones  
Whether planning a new setting or renovating an existing  
tayoul, lhe process is similar, although the relative importance or  
each phase varies according lo the extent or the intervention.  
Planners are usually given a design program which spectlies the  
g\<meral aims and objeetivea or a building as well as its content. The  
content is expressed in terms or gl!neral and speeUlc runctlons. For  
each function, a spatial area is defined and perlormance speclfica-  
lions are outlined, such as the control or Ugbting, temperature,  
desired levels or social Interactions, and ambiance. Wayfinding,  
unfortunately. is rarely Included in performance specifications  
although It certainly is a !unction - one that should not he ignored.  
People finding their way in complex settings will try lo under-  
stand what the setting contains and how ii is organized. In order to  
lorm a mental map or the setting, Ibey have lo identify things to  
map. Among the basic building blocks or cognitive mapping are  
spatial entities. People can only map these spatial entitles ii they  
are distinct, ii they have an identity that distinguishes them lrom  
surrounding spaces.  
Slmilarly. decision making can only be sustained ii destinations  
and lntennedlate sul\>\<lestinallons have an identity distinguishing  
them ll'om other places. The same applies to decision execution. A  
plaoe bas to he recognized before a decision can he transformed into  
behavior. Distinctiveness giving phwes their identity is, thus, a major  
requirement lor waylindlng. This can be achieved by the lorm and  
volume ol the space that delioos srebilOOlural and decorative elements  
and by the use o/ finishes, light. colors. and graphics.  
A rather curious example or distinctiveness Is the house within  
the house or the arehlteetural museum in Frankfurt \(seep. 86\). The  
house within takes on a particular meaning by Its surprise value. It  
also symbolizes the elementary habitat which can he seen as the  
souree or architecture. Meaning and symbolic expression clearly  
**reinforce distinctiveness.**  
Activities and the atmosphere created by people's behavior can  
create a form ol distinctiveness. Although this may be a strong  
!actor to which people are sensitive, it has the disadvantage or not  
being permanent - or disappearing v.1th the now or the key occu-  
pants.Open-air markets. lor example. can give a very strong sense  
or distinctiveness to an urban square or street for the lime of their  
occupation. Once the stands are taken away, however, the square  
might not be reeogni1.able to the casual visitor.  
**Designers have at their disposal a variety of means to create**  
dfstlnctivencss. II is important lo note, though. that users will  
Digitized by Google **In designing a home fOf' a new Department**  
**of Environmental Graphics and Wayflnding**

**of the department should be considered.**

**ing designers and researchers, while the**

**certain knowledge base and design s kills.**  
**The aims and objectives are achieved by**

**tion. vvhich might include atelier work.**  
**courses, seminars, conferences. library**  
\~ices. **and maintenance. It is in respect of**  
**these activities that spaces are identified.**  
**A main reason for the difficulty In under-**  
**standing labyrinths is their absence of spatial**  
**identity. When a person walks through a**  
**uniform labyrinth, each zone looks just the**  
**same as the one visited before. The absence**  
**of spatial identity leaves the user without**  
**building blocks to construct a cognitive map**  
**or to form an overall image of the layout.**

**Excessive zoning laws at the urban scale**

**In the second phase of the planning process. similar functjons tend**  
to be grouped together according to the following rationales\:  
• **The need for human contact or privacy**  
• **The necessity for information exchange**  
• The sharing or certain services  
**This basic grouping in respect to functions establishes a pri-**  
mary order in a complex setting. The bubble diagram and its deri-  
vations are traditional tools for establishing this functional order  
among the required facilities.  
Let us now sl!p into the way!inding shoes or some potential  
users. When visiting a setting, users do not think in terms or lune.  
lions, nor do they have access to a bubble diagram. Users think or  
destinations and see them as specUlc faellllles - a particular omce,  
**a t\)'\)\>C of shop, the washrooms, or \\\\rhatever. Each individual space**  
**in a setting is a potential destination.**  
**Any complex setting has a great number of destinations, and if**  
**it "'·ere not for the grouping of similar destinations into zones it**  
would be very difficult !or people to !ind their way. J ust imagine a  
building, such as a hospital. in which each room was allocated by  
chance. Clearly It would be total chaos. and even With the most  
**Original from**  
86 UNIVERSITY OF MICHIGAN sopblstlcated lnlormation system it would be impossible to guide  
stall, patients, and visitors to their destinations.  
Destination zones operate at dlllerent levels. Let us introduce  
this notion with the help ol an everyday example\: a person shop-  
ping tor a particular food item in an urban context. In order to buy,  
**let's be extravagant, a rare Russian caviar in a complex multi-**  
purpose downtown facility, the person will probably consider the  
shopping area as a fll'St destination zone which distinguishes itself  
from other zones such as office or residential. Within the shopping  
zone, the person will look for a sul\>-zone that groups food products,  
tor example, a supermarket. Even at that level we can consider the  
speciallty counter to be a sul\>-zone of the supermarket. Destination  
zones are therefore composed of sul\>-destlnatlon zones which are  
In tum composed of sul\>-sul\>-destlnation zones.  
Tbe proper articulation of destination zones affects wayfmding  
in two ways. It facilitates the cognitive mapping process by empha-  
sizing the spatial units to be mapped, and it supports the decision-  
making proce88. Looking at decision diagrams, we fmd that in  
complex buildings, "to reach a major destination zone" Is a higbel"  
# order decision.  
It is crucial that waylinders be able to ldentily certain spatial  
characteristics that allow them to group destinations Into zones.  
Like any other grouping or classification, two foroes are at work\:  
Identity and equivalence.  
*/Mntit11.* as we have already outlined, is the characteristic that  
allows us to differentiate one space from another. *Equivalence* is  
**the characteristic th.at allows us to group them into zones along**  
**some common traits. In order to distinguish one destination zone**  
from another, the zones must have distinct features, but the spatial  
units within a zone have to have some common characteristics.  
Tbe spaces needed for a particular function usually share  
certain physical characteristics. All shops, for example, have  
something In common. By grouping spaces according to their  
!unctions, the designer creates destination zones which distinguish  
themsel,·es from other zones.  
Large unifunctional settings, on the other hand, may not have  
**distinguishable destination zones. Visitors tend to be faced with**  
almost insurmountable difficulties In mapping the setting because  
everything looks more or Jess the same. U niformity does not favor  
decision making.  
In a previous chapter we said that spatial planning sets the  
stage for w&\)11nding. The truth of this statement ls brought out  
again. The development of an adequate slgnage system depends on  
a clear ldent1Hcatlon of destination zones. Where else can you  
direct people?  
**In some large commercial complexes. designers have success-**  
**fully introduced .. artificial .. destination zones by giving each zone**  
a particular character that is not ne\<.'essarily related to Its !Unc-  
tion. Metro Centre in Newcastle, England, with Its "three miles or  
spacious shopping malls." over 350 shops. and •tree parking for  
10, 000 cars." Is one of the world's largest commercial complexes.  
Although It Is essentially unifunctlonal, it contains \(and advertises\)

10.3 Circulation patterns and wayftnding  
Each o! the lour types o! circulation systems bas features that  
make it -yfinding-!riendly.  
In this section, we identlly the features that make circulation  
systems easier to map and that !acllitate decision maktng and  
decision execution. Both cognitive mapping and decision maktng  
are allected by the patterns or the circulation systems. These  
patterns have been Identified as follows\:  
• Shoestring  
• Gestalt  
• Systematized  
• Network or repetitive pattern  
**The shoestring pattern. which is based on a random or a quasi ..**  
random distribution, does not have an underlying organizational  
principle. Users would be ill advised to searcb !or something that iB  
not there.  
Mapping **a** sboeatrlng  
Rooallln,g that cognitive mapping ls, by de!lnltlon, a prooess of  
organizing spaces, one may wonder whether people can actually  
map a shoestring arrangement. The answer is that they can. Peo-  
ple are able to map these random arrangements, within certain  
limits, with respect to their size and complexity.  
Probably the most efficient strategy is lo lake note o! land·  
marke and use them as mental anchors. These anchors can then  
**Major events in a** \~rson·s **life are used as**  
**anchor points in time. Most people can**  
**remember even minor things th1t happened**  
**just before or just after major events.**  
Digitized by Google Mopping • -ng **arra"91ment of the**  
**drculoltiO\(Jtiginal from**  
105 UNIVERSITY OF MICHIGAN Cllapter 10  
Spatial planning  
10.3 Circulation patterns and wayfindlng  
serve to attach segments of the route and the surrounding space.  
Anchor points in wayfinding can be distinctive physical features,  
events. and or distinct destination zones.  
Distinctiveness, we have seen. can be acltie\>·ed by outstanding  
features and by compositional characteristics. The repetitions or  
spaces or architectural features, their rbythmlc arrangements, and  
other proportional relationships can be considered distinctive and  
thus gain anchor-point quality.  
\~fcmorable **features or events are not necessarily the same for**  
everybody; what may be a landmark for one person may not for  
another. Again. it is by planning with redundancy that communica·  
**lion will be most effective.**  
**In many cases a path *i.s* structured, but for a variety of rea·**  
sons having to do with the architecture or with the stale of mind  
of the user, the structure is not perceived. In such a case it is  
Weglble. The user will see the path as a shoestring and wW revert  
to the anchor-point technique In attempting to understand and  
cope with It.  
W e therefore recommend that anchor points be Introduced as  
a failsafe device even In the best-planned settings to allow for the  
eventuality that the circulation pattern may *not* be perceived.  
**Mapping 11 scatter**

map the routes Independently. Given that the routes are or the  
shoestring type, anchor points become tbe cognitive structuring  
information.  
This cognltive strategy has limits. as can easily be seen from  
**the c. rowded impNmsion gi\\•en in the illustration. In a complex**  
**scatter-point network it remains quite possible to map certain**  
paths, but not the whole network.

10.S Circulation patterns and wayflodlng  
**Mapping a Geetalt**  
Aooordlng to Gestalt theory, *Prligrumz* l6 a major determining  
!actor ln the perception *ol* linear form. There l6 no precise transla-  
tion *lor Priignanz.* It relers to the ease with wblch a form Imprints  
Itself on the mind. Simplicity \(or goodness or form, as a Gestalt  
psychologist would say\) Is the most Important !actor. Addltlonal  
!actors are symmetries and other regularities in composition.  
It must be realized, however, that the wayfmding public rarely  
sees the overall form ol the paths. Quite the contrary. The Gestalt  
bas to be mentally constructed from partial views.  
Even a form as complex as that or a running figure Is grasped -  
and communicated - mainly through lines and Joints. In buildings  
with Gestalt-type organizations, It l6 suaoested that designers  
emphasize straight or curved lines and, In particular, junctions *or*  
joints or the form.

10.4 P1annlng the layout\: a summary  
The scatter-point network Is composed or shoestring patterns  
and can be undentood only on tbe basis ol anchor points. In a  
network, anchor points should coincide with Intersections. This  
might, in certain cases, lacilita.te a partial understanding In Gestalt

are bard to cope wilb.  
Grid networks reOect a recurring geometric pattern. The  
orthogonal Is the most common grid and is known to be easily  
pereelved and understood. Combined with coordinates, it estab-  
lishes an elllclent addr\<!ss system which is reOected In the decision  
plans of the users.  
Hierarchical networks are less constraining In form than the  
grid. Care, however, bas to be taken to communicate the underly-  
ing organizational principle.  
The layout of a setting has a strong influence on wayfindlng. 01  
course. there is no *ideal* circulation system. What is best for any  
given setting will depend on the function of the setting, its size, and  
Its environmental context. But !or each system a structuring order  
and/or structuring information has to be expressed so that mem·  
bers ol tbe wayllndlng public can develop their decision plans and  
can thus map tbe setting cognitively.

**Paths and vegetation**  
**rn11rt.lng an entrance.**

**11.1 Entrances**  
-·-

# · ·  
# ··

# ..  
**Out-patient entrance**  
**to a large hospital,**  
# --  
# '  
-·l,. --  
\\  
# '  
# -\) \>  
A major hospital in Montreal has one ent rance that looks like a  
**small service. entrance. It is small, but it is certainly not a service**  
**entrance. It is the most direct and most used e-ntrance for out-**  
patients in the hospital, with about a hundred people an hour  
**entering and exiting. Both architectural and graphic communica-**  
tion are almost totally absent. Needless to say, the entrance is  
notoriously bard to !ind.  
The cues indicating the location or a gate or an entrance are  
mainly visual. The blind visitor, having no visual access to the  
building volume or Its ra\~ade, wlll not be able to perceive the archl·  
tectural treatment or the door either. The cues the blind will be  
able to perceive, thererore, will be so much more important to  
**them. A recess. an overhang, landscape features, a decorative**  
**articulation of the entrance. the use of different textures - these**  
arc all cues that can be picked up by the visually impaired.  
In addit.ion, directional guidance can be given by disling\\tisbing  
the access pa th rrom the surrounding area. This may be achie"ed  
through texture or by a guiding shore line. It must be noted that a  
well-articulated path usually has these distinguishing characteris-  
tics In any case. The designer just has to be careful that these cues  
are not exclusively visual.  
Entrances to certain buildings may also be marked by a sound  
**source. This directional cue can be** *\\rery* **efficient as long as it is not**  
drowned out by background noises. It is, or course, nol feasible to  
**have all entrances marked b.\>• a sound; but the solution is a reason·**  
able one !or buildings tha t are frequently used by the visually  
impaired population.  
Sound sources have been designed to be activated by a small  
pocket-size device. This is an interesting solution to elimlnale  
competing sounds and the possible nuisance value or permanent  
sound.  
**The problem of entrances is quite typical in**  
**this hospital. When the hospital was ex-**  
**panded, existing and new buildings were**  
**linked together. The administration has not**  
**been able to decide whether to organize the**  
**hospital as one complel\(. with a main en-**  
**trance, or as a number of semi-autonomous**  
**sections, each with its own entrance. The**  
**situation shown reflects that ambiguity.**  
11.2 Exits  
**The. exit of a buildin g requires particular consideration. Although**  
**it is the same architectural element as an entrance. from the user's**  
**point of view it is certainly not seen the same way. The perception**  
or the exit is olten limited to the actual doors and most or the t ime  
they are seen only a t short range. Exits are important when way-  
fmding- they represent sulH!estinations when people arc leaving  
a setting.  
Digitized by Google  
The wa\)11ndlng tasks or reaching an e ntrance arc quite different  
rrom those !or an exit. For the unJamiliar visitor, the entrance has  
first to be found, while the exit in most settings requires a simple  
return to the point or entry. IC people are able to map their en·  
**trance route, they will require only limited environmental inform.a·**  
**tion to return to the exit. In a recent wayfinding experiment in a**  
labyrinth, we were able to show that most people were able to point

11.2 Exits  
**Besides, it may just be a false alarm, we**  
**convince ourselves.**  
Familiarizing people witb emergency exit routes througb flre  
drills is an option that may work !or some settings but not !or  
others. While it is possible to have fire drills in office buildings, it  
would be virtually impossible to do the same in a large shopping  
complex or an airport. Furthermore, fire drills, like all drills, are a  
nuisance, and people cannot be blamed it they ignore them, al-  
though this attitude might lead to truly catastrophic consequences  
in a real fire.  
A more efficient and elegant solution In public buUdlngs that  
cupe with a high volume or visitors ls to design a number of normal  
entrances/exits which are used In both senses under normal oondi-  
tloos. There Is no reason wby buildings cannot be designed in  
these terms and at the same lime respect constraints imposed by  
security and maintenance. This might be more difficult when  
retrofllling an existing structure, but there Is no doubl that a good  
argument can be made that the security or people *in* the building  
might jusl be more important than lhe security *of* the building.  
The direct peroeplion or exits lrom the inside is only possible lo  
settings with large open spaces such as shopping malls, airports,  
and stadia. The argument here Is to do away with the notlon of fire  
exits as much as possible wbUe still respecting the satety require-  
ments or having alternatlve escape routes.  
**11.3 Paths**  
**In discussing wayfinding communication related lo paths, we**  
should distinguish between two aspects\:  
• Pereeptlon or a path, Its use. and Its accessibility  
• Understanding the oonliguration or the circulation system  
**Let us first consider information on paths - it tells people**  
where the path is, whether it leads to somewhere worthwhile, and  
whether or not they are allowed to take it.

**ci.o.ification of graphic infonnation**  
12.3 Sign typee  
relevant lnlormatlon In the morning Is dlllerent from that which  
should be displayed In the al!ernoon. Monitors In &lrports, or large  
arrival and departure boards. are In a constant state of change  
througbout the entire day.  
Much is made in Chapter 15 of the need to provide wayflndere  
witb the rigbt \(i.e., the most up to date\) information at the rigbt  
time \(i.e., when Ibey need it\), and at the rigbt place \(i.e., where  
they need it\). In highly O uid settings sucb as airports, amusement  
parks, or sports arenas, for example, dynamic signs provide tbe  
obvious answer. In more stable situations, they may still be use!ul,  
but on a much more limited basis.

**Forms of graphic information**  
The purpose of "the graphics" In wayllndlng Is to assist people  
first In the decision-making process and then In the decision-  
executing process, In other words, to help them find their way  
to their destinations along their chosen routes.  
Before we come to the kinds ol graphics Involved In each  
process, In two subsequent cbapters, we deal here with the  
forms that graphic lnlormaUon may take.  
For purposes or slmpllclty, we subdivide these forms Into  
two major groups\: verbal \(or typograpblc\) and non-verbal \(or  
plctograpblc\). Each Is discussed In terms ol bow II ls allected  
by color, layout, and Ughtlng.

are "too big.· The size Ille letter mu11 be \(to be peroeived and  
oomprebended Crom 200 feet away\) will also lnJluence Ille size that  
the sign must be.

Helvetica letterform, whleb, under Ideal condlllo118, yields 50 feet

Inter-word

a sign. the typographer's Ideal or equal word spacing is easy to  
achieve and has the advantages or making the sign look better and  
increasing leglblllty. The space between words should. in our  
oplnlon, be equal to the space occupied by a lov.-er-case "r.·

lnter·letter  
Good practice in signage, as in prin\~ is to vary the amount or  
spacing between letters to compensate lor their shapes and thus lo  
produce the illusion that the spacing is equal. \(In !act, 11 one did nol  
do this, and inserted identical amounis or space between all letters,  
lhe line would appear to be unequally spaced.\)  
The determination or bow much space to put between letters  
\(between two o's, ror example. as d.18tlnct lrom between two l's\)  
used to be done by eye, and craftsmen became very skilled at doing  
It just so. Manufacturers or individual letters \(or "Wes"\) ror sale  
would also Imprint on the masking tape a series or unlts or space.  
An accompanying table indicated how many unlis to place between  
individual letters depending on their juxtaposition, cap to lower  
case, lower case to lower case, lower case to digits, etc. All of this  
**is now done on tbe computer - and frequently not very well. Tbe**  
**principle is, however, the same.13**

successful signage Jetterfonn wlll retain at least 75 percent or Its  
Iegiblllty when distorted 45 degrees. Under such conditions. a on&-  
lncb \(25 mm\) letter ls legible at 35 leet \(10.7 m\).

Two identically si1.ed images, side by side, one in positive \(black on  
white\) lorm, the other in reversed or negative \(white on black\) form  
wlll seem to be dillerent in size. The reversed image wlll appear to  
be up to 10 to 12 percent bigger, bolder, or latter than the other.  
Recent research at Georgia Institute of Technology's College or  
Architecture bas conrlrmed what designers have known from  
experience for some time\: that good "target value· ls one of a  
sign's most valuable characteristics.  
A light-colored sign on a wblte wall ls more dllllcult !or anyone  
to spot than ls a dark sign on the same wblte wall. This being so,  
the cllects or having light-colored letters and symbols on darker  
**fields must be taken into account. The phenomenon involved is**  
called haistion or irradiation because a light image located on a  
dark field wlll seem to radiate and as a result appears to be consid·  
erably larger.

°'""" • Google There are probably as many forms of arrows as there are  
lctter!orms. Some are better than others - "better" In the sense  
that they more e!!ectively communicate direction than do othera.  
Most, however, are just !ancilul.  
**When it comes down to making a statement about which is the**  
"beat" arrow, we just do not know. During the preparation o! work  
!or Expo 67, I \(Paul Arthur\) did some research with locus groups  
and came to the conclusion that an arrow In "1J.lcb the proportion  
of wings to shalt was 1\:2 was beat. Furthermore, the proportion of  
stem thickness to shalt, I was sure. had to be 1\:8.  
Nobody agreed with me and the Expo arrow was never seen  
again. Instead, designers favored !or a long time an arrow with a  
wing-to-shalt relationship o! I \: I, mainly because it lilted so neatly  
in a square and could, as the Expo arrow could not, be rotated  
easily through 360 degrees.  
**The processing of environment.al graphic communication can,**  
however, produce the most bizarre results on occasion. Many  
poople tended to perceive the "square" arrow \(under certaln cir-  
cumstances\) as a chicken footprint moving In the *opposite* d.lroo-  
tion to that which was Intended.  
The chamfering or the ends of the wings \(to reinforce d.lroo-  
tion\), the slight lengthening or the shalt \(to an approximate 7\:9  
rclatiooship\), and the groining o! the area where shaft meets wings  
\(whi ch had been a feature o! the Expo arrow\) all came later, and  
by the mid.SOS many o! us were reasonably sure that *this* was the  
**best arrow.**  
Designers, however, wllh their eyes so lirmJy fixed on form. are  
lrequently blind to content. The form o! the arrow Is one thing and  
whether there wlli ever be agreement on a "beat" form Is unlikely.  
It may not even matter very much. What does matter a great deal Is  
what designers do with t hese arrows.  
***AB* we have already observed earlier in this chapter, the arrow**  
**is a concept-related g1yph representing movement of some sort, but**  
**we frequently are not sure what kind of movement is indicated. I f**  
**the onl\)' movements v.-e ever needed to make v.·ere either to the left**  
or to the righ\~ there would be no problem at all. However, we often  
want to Indicate movement up and down - or straight ahead - and  
here many real wayflndlng problems arise. II the whole world were  
perfectly literate we would advocate dropping arrows altogl!ther  
**and just using words.**  
**Under such circumstances of universal literacy. the v.•ords**  
"straight ahead" would be much clearer than arrows that point  
indiscriminately up *and* down to mean the same things. \(Even  
worse. such arrows *nwy* actually mean upstairs or downstairs. We  
ne-·er know !or sure.\)  
Sometimes, a simple square is used to convey the concept or  
"straight ahead," Indicating that the arrow holds no solution what-  
soever. Is this renlly true? Is It really to be belie-·ed that whlle mnn  
can actually walk on the moon, he cannot design a "straight ahead"  
**sign? Or a non-verbal "push'' or "pull" plate on a door that every·**  
body can understand? \(01 course, beat of all is no sign at all and,

**Forms of gr1phic infonnation**  
1 3.3 Color  
**Colors available for color coding\:**  
• **Red. yellow, and blue**  
• **Orange. green, and purple \(or violet\)**  
• **Brown and** \~ige  
# •Pink  
• **Black, white, and grey**  
**Turquoise is the sort of ambiguous hue th.at**  
**is not available for this purpose because for**  
**the many people who call it •blue,• there is**  
**an equal number who call it .. gre-en .. which,**  
**of course, defeats the whole purpose of**  
**color coding.**  
!or color coding purposes. The *only* colors avallable !or color  
coding purposes are thoso to which a gllnerally agreed-upon name  
can be put - like red or yellow or blue. While there may be a large  
number of tints and shades of these colors, Ibey would still be  
called red, yellow, blue, etc., and because there cannot be tv.'O or  
anything in a color code \(like dark blue and light blue, for exam-  
ple\), there are still only nine individual colors. There just aren't  
enough colors - and if there were, the memory load extends to no  
more than five at most, alter which people can neither remember  
nor distinguish between the colors used.  
Another problem Is the lmpraetlcality of color coding anything  
transient. II graphic design Is yellow, and fashion is blue, for exam·  
pie, whal happens when they move to another area? Uthe color  
**oode manifested itself in painted doors. tbc problem is insurmount-**  
able. Even if it is only the signs that are colored, the problems of  
stocking so many signs in dlllerent colors could be one sort of  
nightmare while switching the signs about could well be another.  
A way around this problem, however, ls to color code by areas,  
not by function. Thus a complex, iargll, square bullding might be  
subdivided Into quadrants, each of which had a color assigned to It  
permanently, lrre\$pective of which departments or functions  
occupied each. Graphic design could move from the yellow to the  
blue quadrant with no need to change anything. If one is going to  
apply a color code in Ibis way, then it should always be on a geo-  
**graphic, and never on a functional. basis.**  
But then there is still the matter of fading. Say five years later  
one has to match colors of new signs to those of the orlglnals. It Is  
most unllkely that this can be done. The old signs will have faded  
and, as a result, one will have tv.• o blues, two greens, and so on.  
A lar more practical approach ls to consider the colored lines  
which are brlefiy discussed In section 13.2.5. Tapes In lndMdual  
colors should be thought of as so many highways routed through  
the complex. One color might, for example, link together entrances,  
elevators, stairs, and perhaps washrooms. Small signs in verbal  
and non-verbal form along the route could indicate the direction of  
the closest stair, elevator, or washroom.  
Another color could lead to inter--00noected buildlngs whlle two  
others could pick up designated departments or lacllitles. As long  
as people always knew which lacllitles were on each of the colored  
routes, with small directional signs along the way to help,  
wayflndlng could be mucb slmplilied and the number of signs  
reduced.  
The question of where the lines should be placed is always  
raised. Everyone prefers the fioor - like Hansel and Gretel in the  
forest. But this is rarely a practical solution. The lines wear out or  
are obscured by objects placed afterwards In the corridors - or just  
by people. The celling ls better and, when It can be accompllshed,  
eye level on the wall Is best or all.

t3.3 Color  
A final point on the selection ol colors lor signs, one which  
**makes the task even more restrictive. i8 that there are certain**  
colors that should be reserved lor public safety uses only. These  
are red, yellow, and green.  
13.4 Layout  
There is a rule or thumb to the ellect that no signS should ever  
contain more than live or six messagl!6 or "bits" or information, or  
which the sign Itself ls one. There Is. however, some latitude In the  
meaning of "bits.·  
On a blgllway sign, bits are single lines. Thus a sign for motor-  
ists' use should contain no more than three lines ol copy. The kind  
or Information intended for pedestrian use, however. may be more  
extensi\\'tl. There would be little value, for example. to the averagoe  
building directory *U* it could only display three or lour lines or  
information. The information required at decision points, in the  
form or directional signs. will lrequently also run to much more  
than three or lour bit&.  
It Is Important to remember In tbls connection bow we "read"  
signs. We don't read signs the way we read print. There our habits  
are very structured\: we read consistently and sequentially left to  
rlgbt \(In Roman orthography\) and top to bottom. Signs, however, in  
an unlamillar environment, are read In the same way we read the  
rest ol the selling\: using a scanning and glancing process that is  
akin to a person looking for "meaning" In a non-objective work or  
art. \(See section 6.2.2 lor a description or this process or scanning  
and glancing.\)  
Thus we read signs In a relatively unstructured ""'Y· as a  
series of glances. \(This is at the root of wily people so frequently  
"read" signs for what they lhln.k they should say, rather than what  
they do say, as we saw In section 13.1.3.\)  
This glancing process requires that lolormatlon on dlrectional  
signs be somehow grouped into smaller units of, say, three lines  
each when large amounts or information need to be displayed.  
As the illustrations on the following page show, three columns  
of three messllfl"8 side by side are easier to assimilate than a  
single column of nine. Alternatively, If space requirements demand  
a vertical sign, the information Is equally acooptable as three  
groups, of three messages each, arranged vertically.  
Even better, when space permits in directional signs, is the  
separation or the groups into two signs - with those that lie on the  
viewer's left in one sign and those on his or her right in another.  
II is still, however, desirable that information be displayed in  
groupings ol no more than three or lour messages each.

**Forms of graphk\: information**  
**13.5 Wuminatioo of Sign!\>**  
**From a purely practical standpoint. s igns are**  
**the cause of many of the problems that**  
**beset people trying to find their way in**  
**unfamiliar settings. They affect able.bodied**  
**and disabled people alike - but consider the**  
**extent to which faulty signage exacHbates**  
**the problems of the disabled.**  
Digitized by Google  
It must be noted that some gloss on signs Is unavoidable. Even  
a surlaoo that appears flat or totally laeking in sheen when Wuml·  
nated and viOWlld at an angle near to the perpendicular will actu-  
ally appear somewhat glossy when the angle or view shifts to near  
the grazing angle ol 85 degrees off the perpendicular. In other  
words, angular distortion creates the appearance of gloss on  
coating\>; or materials that are inherently flat.  
Thus a satin finish will appear to be eggshell or even semi-gloss  
when viOWlld at a grazing angle, and semi-gloss finish will appear  
to be full-gloss under the same oonditions.  
On the other band, there Is the oompUcatlon ol vandalism.  
Glossy surlaoos. which decrease legibility, also discourage vandal·  
ism and are easy to clean; matte surlaoos, wblcb enhance legibility,  
may encourage vandalism and are difficult to clean. So it ls a  
**tra.deoff, but ooncem for vandalism is not a valid argument in favor**  
of shiny signfaecs.  
I . Ambiguity  
Although a message might be quite clear to the person who origi·  
nally put the sign up, it may be wholly unclear to the obscl'\\·er.  
2. Coalllct  
Bits of conflieting information in a sign \(or in two signs close  
together\) create dillicullies. This happens particularly when new  
signs are installed and the old ones are not removed. Or It may be  
the result or two signs that complemented eaeb other at one tlme,  
but that oonfilct in new circumstances.  
3. Deftclency  
**Too little information is just as bad as too much. A sign must**  
contain all the inlormalion that a person needs at that particular  
plaec.  
4\. Exce84  
Conluslon generally results when more data than a person needs ls  
provided at a given decision point.  
5. Glare  
**Signs are much more efficient when they are well illuminated.**  
**Sometimes a sign can be well illuminated from some standpoints**  
but obscured by reOected ligbt lrom others. Signs plaeed opposite  
windows, !or instance, can end up reOecting the outside. cars  
moving on the street, passersby, and so on.  
6. DleglbWty  
Messages that are illegible are those that are too small to be seen  
\(or recognized\) lrom the reading distance or the sign, even thougb  
there may be nothing wrong with the message. Readability and  
legibility are not the same.  
184  
**Original from**  
UNIVERSITY OF MICHIGAN 7.h\>accuracy  
II is essential to provide all the Information that people need, when  
they need it. But It must be the right information - up-to-date, and  
aoourate.  
8. Obstructions  
There is a constant jockeying for position in the environment and  
things end up obscuring or blocking the view of other things. Some  
things don't matler, but when a planter or Ugbt fixture or piece of  
HVAC equipm ent is placed in front of a sign, it does matter.  
9\. UnrellabWty  
Most people are prepared to depend on signs in an unlamtliar  
setting, but only for as long as they are dependable and do not, for  
no apparent reason, suddenly let them down.  
10\. Us  
Finally, we are not alwsys able to give our lull attention *to* the  
wayfmdlng taak. We are eaaily distracted. AB a reeult, we may mlss  
a crucial piece of information, a si8Jt, a landmark, an architectural  
feature we were told to turo J ell at, or whatever. A carelully  
planned wayltndlng system Is forgiving In the sense that we are not  
hopel essly and forever lost because we missed that laat cue, sign,  
or signal.

**Graphic information for decision making**  
People need graphic Information In order to formulate an  
action plan with respect to wayflndlng In an unfamlllar setting,  
In other words, for decision making. The graphic Information  
they need Involves orientation and general Information about  
the setting. The type of Information needed once an action plan  
bas been developed and Is In the process of execution Is the  
subject of Chapter 15.

**14.J OrlentatJon**  
Digitized by Google  
Experience demonstrates that the 60-\<legree oblique view Is  
prelerl'OO by most people because It Is regarded 88 more realll;tlc.  
However, such views introduce the problems of perspective and  
**cannot be used to measure distances \(or even to indicate them on**  
any sort or scale\).  
Whether this prelerence for maps which include photographic  
information \(even though modified\) is soundly based or merely  
subjective must be determined by others. However, it is safe to say  
that the more "realistic" a map of an exterior setting is, the more  
efficient It will be.  
And. or course. It must be remembered that orientation or  
alignment to the setting Is cruclal. This will help to compensate !or  
the map·s shortcomings which, 88 we have already noted, are Its  
verticality and its two dimensionality, Irrespective or what sort or  
**map it is.**  
Interior settings  
Rather different rules apply for interior settingl!. Axonomelric and  
perspective views are rarely as helpful 88 they are out.side. A higb-  
er degree ol stylization \(than is desirable in exterior maps\) is not  
only acceptable but Is likely to be helplul 88 well.  
At the same time. paradoxically, It must be noted that people  
have more dl!Dculty finding their way about In Interior spaces,  
where they are deprived of the kinds or cues they find so useful in  
the outdoors. In section 15.1, we present the reader with some  
concepts in floor and room numbering systems that are helplul to  
wayfmdcrs.  
Wayfmders often have a very difficult time when they are  
conl'ronted wllh too much information \(or, or course, too little\).  
Almost any map tends by its very nature to contain large amounts  
or Information. To digest or sort through thls can be a problem In  
ltseU and It Is only compounded when the visitor Is presented with  
several maps In one locatlon \(each of a dlllerent floor or level in  
the building\).  
When laced with such a "choice of maps," visitors may become  
confused and ignore the maps altogether. A far better practice is  
not lo try to do everything at once, but to take things a step at a  
lime\:  
• **Provide visitors with level numbers for all the tenants in the**  
complex, listed alphabetically.  
• Oel visitors to the correct level for the tenant they have come to  
see.  
• Provide, on each level, a map \(of that level\) to show where the  
tenants are actually located, with their addresses.  
• Ensure that this map Is properly aligned with the floor It.sell.

**Graphic information for decision executing**  
Having successfully formulated their action plans \(see Chap-  
ter 14\), visitors to an unfamlllar setting wW need dlllerent  
types of Information to assist them In getting entclenUy to  
their cbo\<len destinations. Unfortunstely, however, few ol  
these "types ol lnformatlon" bave ever been standardized and,  
as a result, they are called dlllerent thing& In different bulld·  
lngs.  
**Floor level names are an example of this. ls the Door on**  
wblcb a person enters a bul1d1ng from the street called "main,•  
**or "'ground," or "'lobby," or .. 1," or somethlng else again? And**  
what If Ibis same building bas ma\)or entrances not \)ust on  
one level, but on two? *Are* each or these also called "ground"  
or "main," etc? And If not, then bow are they dlstlnguJsbed?  
How are Ibey Identified on elevator control buttons? Room  
numbering Is another area where, apparently, anything goes.  
Is there such a thing as a system? And If so, wW It work on  
open-landscape Doors as well as on tbo\<le with conventional  
corridors? Where do signs "go"? ls there a point to standardlz·  
Ing tbls, or Is It good enough to continue the practice of putting  
them wherever they can conveniently be Otted lo?  
These and other Issues dealt with lo this chapter are, we  
feel, very Important and, on all ol them, we bave reoommenda·  
!Ions to make. On the other band, there are many w\~s ol  
doing things, some neither better nor worse than others. It Is  
therefore less Important that our recommendations be adopted  
than !bat out ol them there should emerge an agreement  
among practitioners and bul1d1ng owners and managers Iba!  
standardization Is desirable.

UNIVERSITY OF MICHIGAN 15.1 Floor and room numbering systems  
It Is rrequently a source or surprise to designers to learn just bow  
many people do not know \(without being told\) that room \(or office\)  
I 115 is on the I Ith Door. This is one orthose things designers and  
building man&glll'S take for granted without having any reason ror  
doing so.  
Another is the ideotilicalioo of Door levels themselves. North  
Americans have not so far developed a coordinated, universally  
accepted method or Identifying levels. from the parking or other  
levels below grade, through the ground or main Door \(which may or  
may not be at grade all around the building perimeter\). to the other  
Doors above.  
The general practice Is to start al grade and ealJ It something  
\(M or G or I or something else\) and then to work *dQWrl* through the  
Doors below and *up* through those above. This is all very well for  
buildings built on level ground but becomes a problem when the  
building is on the side of a slope or is connected to another building  
by an abov&ostreet pedestrian walkway.  
In such cases, there are two levels al which we can gain access,  
one higher than the other. Thus, in addition to what the owners  
\(and their architects\) think of as the "main" entrance, there Is  
another one al level 2 \(or even 8\).  
Now lmaglne going to a cUnlc on level 2 in a hospital built into  
the side of a steep slope. We enter on level 3 \(although we are not  
necessarily conscious or this on entering\). Which elevator button  
do we press? U p, of course, when we should be going down. More  
frustration. More stress.

*·c\:* The meaning ol the word "concourse" may be weU enough

in a downward direction. A different sort of problem arises in

Is at grade with two more levels above It and. say. three more  
below. How. under such circumstances. should one label the levels?  
Reentering the garage at P3 as a pedestrian to retrieve one's  
vehicle i s not helpful.

Thoughts on control panel nomenclature\:  
The star has gained wide currency as a  
tactile symbol for the Ground f loor. so why  
not u\~ It as a substitute for .. Main" or .. 1 ..  
or '"Ground" - particularty if it is explained  
in a panel close by, as shown in the illustra-  
tion on the opposite page.  
If the concept of levels below grade being  
identified as - 1, -2, and so on, is accepted,  
t here is still a need to explain the meaning  
of these symbols. Obviously "One below" is  
better than "'Minus one ... If one accepts the  
logic of the star as a kind of zero, then it  
follows that there should be a 1 above it and  
a -1 below it. even though North Amerkan  
custom is generally in favor of the level  
above Ground being identified as 2.  
Some researchers prefer SUB1, SUB2. etc .• for  
these below-grade floor levels. 1 A determi-  
nation should, however, be made as to  
which is the best solution and to stay w ith it.  
Note the explanation of the fact that there  
are two. not one, main entrances to one of  
these buildings\: one on the Ground floor  
and ano&f9iltil\\ Vr'J'nlhird level.

Di9itiR•by Google \(98 UNIVERSITYOFMICHIGAN Open-1And8C&pe Doors  
Not all Doors have corridors in the conventional sense. They may,  
for example, be made up of open space with dividers to create  
offices. While there are still corridors of a sort, they are not nearly  
as evident as they are when the walla go from Door to ceiling.  
The whole idea of open-landscape interiors ls to acknowledge  
and to facilitate change. This has led us to the conclusion that  
room numbering in such cases ls lmpoaslble. Instead, we have  
lound It uselul to create a series ol clearly Identified zones based  
on the spaces dellned by the columns. The complete or partial  
layout of a Door can change radlcally lrom time to time to accom-  
modate changing work habits or the growth of a department that  
NqUires relocation of personnel.  
By imposing an alpha-numeric grid over all the levels of the  
building in the normal way \(with letters on one axis and numbers  
on the other\), a consistent malrix is created. Thus the spaces \(or  
zones\) defined by the columns are identllled as Al , Bl, Cl \(or A2,  
B2, C2\) in one direction and Al, A2, A3 \(or Bl, 82\. 83\) In the other.  
This generally results In three to n"e persons sharing an "ad-  
dreas." *U* each ol them ls Identified by name at the entrance to  
their work space, a visitor coming to see one or them will experi·  
ence no dUllculty.  
As often as not, Doors in modern office buildings are a mixture  
of offices with regular partitions and work stations with dividers.  
This zone system works equally well here - even though ii ls ln8'1-  
table that some of the columns will be inside offices and, therefore,  
cannot be used to display the addreases.

**Designers and architects are making increasing use of landmarks**  
as part of integrated wayfmding aystems for a wide variety of new  
projects, from underground pedestrian walkways to airports,  
hospitals, and college buildingS. Generally speaking, landmarks  
perform two principal wayfmdlng !unctions\:  
• They help visitors to get their bearings. as the Eillel Tower does  
**in Paris, for example.**  
• They help staff lo provide directions to vtsilors, such as, "To get  
to my office, take the elevator to the third Door, go left, and  
continue on till you can see the drinking fountain. My office is  
Just opposite."  
These two examples help to demonstrate the tact that landmarks  
have very real and ellective uses, and that they can be virtually  
anything from a distinctive exterior structure to an interior drink·  
Ing fountain. Their wayfindlng usefulness is enhanced in interior  
spaces particularly when, as is so often the ease, the space is  
relatively bland or uniform. Many college buildings. for example,  
**consist of anonymous undifferentiated spaces connected by simi-**  
**larly anonymous corridors.**

**found in sKtion 10.3.2 where landmarks are**  
**also referred to as anchor points.**

**on column fact. \(ft Is.**  
**of course. the spaas**

15.2 Landmarks  
The placing or physical objects to act as landmarks within build·  
**ings creates an invaluable wayfmding toot The more bizarre and**

anoe lrom stall at the Idea or dJ.recting visitors In a hospital to •go

wrong with that? Surely in a hospital or all places, anything that  
makes people smile \(or giggle\) cannot be all bad.  
**Because, in many of our cities. we are so used to thinking of**

**In new construction. we would go so far as**

15.3 Location of signs  
In executing a decision plan, visitors are asked lo make many  
decisions *en* route to their destinations. The !ewer decisions they  
have to make, the more e!Dclent lbe route.  
**It is important that routes to destinations be the same ror the**  
able bodied and !or persons in wheelchairs.  
**Decision points manifest themselves mainly as corridor inter·**  
**sections and it is here that visitors must have assistance - fre-**  
quently in lbe form or signs.  
As the Wustrations show, we strongly recommend as good  
wayllndlng practice the use or lour signs per lntersecllon. Irrespec-  
tive of Its con!\(guratlon. This ensures maximum vlsibWty or this  
vital information !or visitors coming from all directions. The need  
to provide "the rigtit information at the rigtit place" \(which is what  
every wayfmding designer attempts to do\) is thus !ulftlled.  
**But what of the content? How much information can usefully be**  
provided in these signs? Most cities that permit exterior directional  
signs to important destinations at all, permit them only within a  
given radius o! lhe destination Ilse!!. Thus the hopeless effort or  
trying to provide. at every street corner, d!rectlons lo every desti-  
nation in the city Is a"olded. The same d!sclpllne must be applied  
to Interior signs. It is a hopeless and seU-de!eating task to attempt,  
**at 0\\'ery intersection, to provide directions to 0\\1 cry location in the**  
**building. \\Ve ha\\'e seen this actually done, and the effect is nothing**  
**less than chaotic.**  
What sort or information, then. should be included in signs at  
intersections? II the concept or destination zones \(see section 10.1\)  
has been incorporated into the planning or the buUding. the answer  
is obvious and the results will be efficient.

15.3 Location or sign•  
The main problem with directional signs is that if they are to be the  
least bit effective, they must be totally reliable, ln the sense that  
wayfmders must be able to follow a trail or series or signs, located  
at reassuring Intervals, to their chosen destination. This Is why It  
is so important that planners select with care what destinations  
they are gulng to provide directions to.  
Once any destination Is featured on a dlrectional sign at a  
given Intersection, there Is an obllgatlon to include it on every  
subsequent directional sign until the destination iB reached. This is  
further substantiation for the concept or providing long-distance  
directions !or gross areas or destination zones but strongly re-  
stricting the distances al which Individual destinations \(our "rai-  
sins" in a previous metaphor\) fll'St appear on directional signs.  
**15.4 Placement of** signs  
**We make a distinction between •tocating ..**  
**and •placing• signs. The former has the**  
**sense of location on plan. while the latter**  
**term refers to placement in elevation.**  
A 1.75 Inch \(45 mm\) cap-height message is  
displayed on. 3 inch *as* mm\) "blade" or  
**message module. F ive such blades total 15**  
inches \(380 mm\).  
We have long cherished a dream that at some time ln the future the  
entire world wtll acknowledge the need to ensbrlne graphic  
wayllndlng lnlormatioo ln a horizontal band. exactly 16 lnches \(400  
mm\) deep and positioned 47 inches\(1200 mm\) oil the ground \(or  
Door\). This iB VisuCom's lnfoBand I. It should be restricted exclu-  
sively to wayfmding Information and therefore should be free of  
thermostats, light switches, and other distractions \(although it is  
proper that lire alarms should be located wltbln It\).  
lnloBand 1 bas been restricted to the level betwoon 47 to 63  
inches above the noor \(120 *to* 160 cm\) !or two reasons\:  
t. This level fits more or less comfortably wltbln the oooe or vision  
of pedestrians, both adults and children, and or people In wheel-  
chairs. It is designed to acoommodate as many people as possi-  
ble. including those with low vision who must be able to get very  
close to a sign in order to read it. It also ensures reliability in  
terms of where users should expect to fmd a sign. the absence  
of whlch bas a devastating effect on wayllnders.  
2. This level may extend no more than 16 inches \(400 mm\) in  
height, allowing for no more than five lines of type. This Is In  
keeping with the wayfmding principle that no more than five  
lines or "bits" of information should be displayed on any one  
sign and with the recommendation that the cap-heigllt or the  
typeface used should be t.75 lncbes \(45 mm\). Under such re-  
strictions designers must be extremely selective in the wording  
or the messages.  
One can only speculate on what a different world it would be  
for wayfinders in unfamiliar settings if this principle of a band,  
sacred to the display of information, were to be established In  
every building, everywhere.

15.5 Equipment  
Tele\<\:ommunlcattons Devices for tbe Oea! \(TOils\) enable hearing -  
**or speech·irnpaired persons to communicate by translating the**  
**sounds they cannot hear *or* make into visual signals on a screen** -  
or as hard copy on paper.  
A TDD keypad is used to type verbal messages \(in, say, a per-  
son ·s home\) which are transmitted via telephone lines to another  
TDD terminal \(in, say. an office somewhere\) providing that the  
other address. in addition to Its regular telephone number. also bas  
a TDD number. It can then re\<\:eive such a call.  
This equipment Is, of course, interactl\\•e in that it transmits  
dialog between the parties involved. It is relatively inexpensive and  
now there are portable versions weighing not mueh more than a  
couple of pounds.  
The role of TOils as W11yfinding devices to speech- and hearing -  
impaired persons is obvious. By enabling them to call abead and  
thereby obtain the kinds of information they need about a setting  
**or the services it offers, they are much more likely to make the**  
journey than they would be *U* this information were not so readily  
available.

15.5 Equipment  
Most of the time when we talk about wayfinding, we are dis-  
**cussing answers to well-defined questions such as, .. How do I. in**  
this particular location within Ibis overall setting, get to my desli·  
nation, namely the such-and-ouch?" But what *U* lhe location of lhe  
defltlnatlon ls *Ml* well defined? Or what *U* it ls not defined at all? In  
other words, what *U* a slgnlllcant part of the wayllnding equatlon Is  
missing?  
For example, what happens when a person arrives for the Orsi  
time at, say, a oommunlty oollege campus oomprlslng some four or  
five buildings to attend a oourse of lectures - but has no Idea  
whatsoever where the l ecture that evening will be held? Imagine,  
too, that several hundred courses are offered every day by this  
# insUtution.  
Assuming the person has been lucky enough to ascertain from  
someone who knows !hat lhe class ls in room 420 in ·A· Bullding at  
7 o'clock, we return to the relatively simple buslne88 of providing  
directions to Bulldlng A. then to lhe fourth Door, and finally to  
room 20 on that Door.  
But what *ll* tbe student bad not bad the good fortune to meet  
someone who knew where the class would be held? Trusting to  
luck is not good enough and this is where touch·screen Interactive  
equipment of some kind can provide all the information a person  
oould want in these or similar circumstances.  
The often used argument that only one person at a time can  
use such equlpmen\~ whereas many people can look at sign stmul·  
taneously, ls perfectly valid. However. the type of information we  
are discussing here could not be displayed on a sign for a wide  
variety of reasons. The room In which a class ls held may well vary  
from week to week. Several hundred different cJasees are *ottered*  
every day of the week and any sign displaying them all would be  
Impossibly huge and also Impossible to read.  
The alternative to the obvious Impossibility and Impracticality  
of blllboard·slz.ed signs is an information desk with a knowledgl\>-  
able attendant In charge. However, the same objection applies\: the  
attendant can deal with only one person at a time. Besides, the  
chance of there being more than one s1&11ed information deek ls  
extremely remote, whereas small ln!\<Hdosks of the type we are  
recommending can be strategically located tbrougbout lhe setting.  
What Schlossberg and others have made possible is the reallza·  
lion of a dream that one day accurate up-to-date Information oould  
be made available to people on a highly personal, t&ilor-made  
basis, specific to their particular needs at a parlicular time.  
Although the manufacturers of electronic directories are apt lo  
make extravftgllllt claims for what their products will do, there is  
no questlon that this equipment solves lhe agll-Old problem of  
containing vast amounts of ln!ormatlon while displaying only as  
much as a viewer can absorb at any given ttme.

UNIVERSITY OF MICHIGAN By means of a touch screen or keypad, users may scroll  
through the directory, which provides names and room numbers  
of the building's tenants and sometimes other Information as well.  
Moreover, and even better, these directories display this infor-  
mation at eye level and not high above our heads. The degree of  
contrast \(between the type, usually orange, and the background,  
generally black\) may be Inadequate for low-vision people, but tbe  
slze of letters \(at about onEHlalt lnch \(15 mm\) ls quite adequate.  
Only one person at a ti.me can make use or such a directory, a  
serious limitation, but one that Is shared with all other types of  
**interactive equipment, and, as we have observed before, only one**  
**person can talk to an attendant at an information desk.**  
The most interesting of these directories make use of fiat, not  
curved, plasma TV screens that require, to all intents, no depth at  
all as they can be accommodated wltbln the thickness \( 4 inches or  
100 mm\) or tbe average wall  
The most typical signal light Is the one that goes on \(usually red or  
green or white\) over the elevator door to signal the car's arrival  
and, In the case of green or red Ugbts, to Indicate to those walling  
the direction or Its travel\: green for up, red for down.  
Another valuable but rare use for signal lights Is to alert the  
deal that an alarm has rung. Visual fire alarms used to consist of a  
pulsing strobe light which was guaranteed to get everybody's  
attention. Recent research, however. has demonstrated that while  
a pulsing low-wattage light could be useful, strobes tend to be  
frightening and may cause temporary blindness in some people.

**16.1 lnformalion desks**  
Different circumstances having to do with the particular func-

Digitized by **Google**  
With the advent of electronic directories \(see section 15.5.4\), the  
information desk and its attendant have in most types of bulldings  
gone the way of tellers in banks with the widespread introduction  
some years ago of automatic teller machines \(ATMs\).  
Visitors looking !or a human source or Information now nod, as  
we observed in the preceding section, security guards who may, or  
very well may not, be prepared to provide wayfinding information.  
In any case, because the prime responsibility of these person·  
nel is security, it is extremely unlikely that they will have been  
exposed to any sort of training in dealing with visitors' questions.  
much less in helping people with impaired speech, hearing, or  
sight.  
In wayfinding terms. this ls regrettable because, as we have so  
frequently stated throughout this book, wayflnding Information is  
not Umlted *to* architecture and signs. It ls also verbal and aural. We  
know that speech ls actually the preferred medium lor most per-  
fectly able-bodied people to receive certain types or information -  
particularly that type which used *to* be \\be main purpose lor having  
an information desk in the ftrst place, namely, to provide directions  
to destinations. If given the choice, most of us would much prefer to  
be *told* how to get there, rather than having to figure ii out from a  
directory, a sign. or even a map.  
For one thing, when you ask directions or people. you can get  
an idea of whether or not they know what they're talking about  
\(and therefore whether or not their inlormatlon Is reliable\). This  
you cannot do with signs and maps which, you may have learned  
**from experience. may or may not be. trustworthy sources o r infor-**  
**mation. A person can explain to you in detail \(and m ore than once,**  
**if necessary\) how to get to a give-a destination and can trace a**  
route on a map which you can take with you. These are things no  
static sign could ever be expected to do.  
All or these things are, admittedly. precisely what sophisticated  
interactive video systems are designed *to* do - right up to and  
including providing the '1sitor v.1th a printed map. The point is tha t  
this form of interael1on, whether with a Jive human being or with a  
machine, Is a desirable part or obtaining directional information.  
II information is to be imparted in the old-fashioned way,  
**person to person, it should be done in such a way as to take into**  
**account that visitors may have articulation, bearing, seeing, or**  
understanding problems. In other words, attendants should be  
trained to deal with these disabUitles in an understanding manner.  
They should. !or example, enunciate each word clearly. look vis\~  
tors squarely In the lace wblle talking, and never put their hands to  
their mouths \(or llddle with their moustaches\) while they are  
talking. They never know when they may be talking to someone

The content and form of an international symbol vocabulary for

Various materials have been used to make tactUe maps.

**Pro and** con  
What should *or* should not be done to assist the sight impaired in  
getting about with greater facility?  
Tactile signs are very rarely installed anyv.ilere. Why is this? ls  
it because of ignorance? ls ii tbe dUficulty \(and added expense\)  
involved in their manufacture? Or ls it because blind people don't  
**want, or need, them?**  
Tactile signs are more expensive to manuracture tban en-  
graved *or* screen-printed signs \(and considerably more than com-  
puter-\<\:ut vinyl legends\). and yes. blind and low-vision people can  
and do use them when they are available \(although. as noted  
elsewhere. they find capital letters much easier to read than lower  
case letters\). Well, what about the other issues? Is there any objec-  
tion to their use? The Canadian National Institute for the Blind  
\(CNIB\) takes the rationalist view that anything that helps is good.  
and it is good because it is helpful. In the United States, on the  
other hand, opinion is sharply divided with one side \(represented  
by the American Foundation for the Blind and the National Councll  
of the Blind\) being of a view slmllar to that of the CNUl, whereas the  
other side \(represented by the National Federation or the Blind\) is  
violently opposed, malntalnlng that "enriching" environmental  
communication especially ror blind people \(the micro-approach\) is  
a clear signal that they cannot get about \(or earn a living\) without  
**these devices. And inasmuch as very fe-w environments are already**  
furnished with special signs and signals for the blind, employers  
**have an obvious '"excuse'" not to hire them.**  
This may well prove to be a dispute for which there ls no satis-  
factory solution but the evidence that blind persons perform well ln  
orientation and even directional situations \(see section 8.3.1\) is  
not, It seems to us, an argument against having tactual identifica-  
tion signs and direetlonal signs.

219 UNIVERSITY OF MICHIGAN Postscript  
**On the emergence of a new kind of professional**  
Digitized by Google  
What did the flrst signs look like? Broken branches on the hunting  
paths of prehistoric man? Piles of roeks guiding nomadic tribes 10  
their next camp? Maybe even earlier than that - claw marks on  
tree bark. or scent messllll'lS. the keys for wblch have been lost  
long ago.  
We know a little bit more than this about e.rchite\<\:ture and  
wayfinding. Already in antiquity, wayfinding design was practised  
for some settings\: labyrinthine spatial layouts were specifically  
designed to disorient people. The purpose was tv.'O!old\: to protect  
**some treasured or sacred objects Crom thieves, or to protect a**  
**place or m•en a city rrom invaders.**  
The power of the labyrinth was physical - to disorient people  
and get them lost. It was also psychological - to Induce a feeling of  
danger and fear.  
Today we are rediscovering wayflnding and wayfindlng design,  
not to bury sacred crocodiles as the old Egyptians did in the laby-  
rinthine pathways within their pyramids, nor to confuse invaders  
**of our cities as was done. in some urban centers of antiquity, but to**  
**assist the wayfinding person in the built environment. an environ·**  
**ment that bas become more complex and often more confusing**  
than anything the Grrek historian Herodotus wrote about in his  
**descriptions of the ancient world.**  
Assisting people in Ibis context means making the built envi-  
ronment accessible. wayfinding ef!lclent, and sale for all - Lnclud·  
ing people with temporary and permanent disabllllles.  
Throughout this book, we have made It clear that wayflnding  
design Is not the antllhesis of interesting design. Quite the reverse.  
**It provides the designer and planner with excelJent reasons to**  
**develop interesting spatial organizations. to articulate settings.**  
**and to communicate interest to the user.**  
**Because wayfinding design comprises basic issues such as**  
**accessibility and safety, it covers large areas or concern, some or**  
which pose difficult questions involving socie\~ choices.  
Wayfinding design is based on wayflndlng behavior. This  
implies that designers need to know the basics about en,1roomen·  
\~ perception and cognition\: they need to know about de\<\:islon  
maldng and spatial problem soMng.  
In addition, it is ne\<\:essary to understand and to know about  
**v.rayfinding behavior in specific situations such as emergencies, in**  
**specific settings such as underground facilities, and for particular**  
groups such as the visually impaired.  
Wayfinding design is also a skill - some might call it an art. II  
involves landscape design, urban design, e.rchilectural, Interior,  
industrial, and graphic design.  
220  
**Original from**  
UNIVERSITY OF MICHIGAN Wayflndlng design Is now recognized as a major Issue and one  
may weU ponder the best policy !or Its practice. It ls quite clear  
that architects. urban designers, landscape designers, and environ-  
mental graphic designers should know the rudiments or wayflndlng  
design. This can only be assured If It Is Incorporated Into educa-  
tional curricula. Professional t raining, In the long term, Is our best  
guarantee. This book has been partly conceived with this use in  
# mind.  
**It must be remembered that architects have many issues to**  
consider when planning a project. Although they ought to know  
about wayflndlng design, they may not themselves be a ble to do it  
**justice in the development of complex settings. They use structural**  
and mechanical engineers. Why should they not use wayfindlng  
specialists as weU ?  
Tbe time has come to start thinking along those lines.

Anatomy of a public building complex  
Just as wayllndlng inlormatloo can be broken down Into Its compc\>-  
nent parts \(see Chapter 12\), It is useful to think or building com-  
plexes as comprising a number of components as well. This makes  
everything more manageable.  
We have round it useful to divide the average building complex  
into fJVe zones \(or component parts\) and lhree circulation systems.  
as follows\:  
The Ove zones\:  
• parkJng lots or gar8gl!1l  
• entrance\(s\) to the building  
• main lobby  
•communal lacllltles  
•departments, omces. tenants  
The three clrculatlon systems\:  
• vertieal circulation  
•horizontal circulation  
•egress throughout the building
